If Ritual Officers, when treating yang illnesses, intentionally use yang talismans, or for yin illnesses use yin talismans, or employ leftward destruction when rightward generation is required, thereby harming others, they shall be sentenced to nine years of penal servitude. If no clear retribution has occurred and the distinction between yin and yang is unclear, this does not apply.

18. If Ritual Officers recklessly add or remove words when performing rituals, thereby slandering their peers, they shall be sentenced to five years of penal servitude. If they debate the rightness or wrongness of public matters or dispute orthodox methods, this does not apply.

19. If Ritual Officers recklessly transmit orthodox methods to unworthy persons for personal gain, their lifespans shall be reduced by twelve years.

20. Ritual Officers of the seventh rank shall record merits but not transgressions. If they submit memorials knowing their rank and yet commit offenses, their lifespans shall be reduced by six years.

21. If Ritual Officers treat orthodox methods as playthings, they shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude.

22. If Ritual Officers, when petitioned by the people for talismans or ritual water, recklessly obstruct due to personal difficulties, they shall be sentenced to six years of penal servitude. If they are petitioned for talismans but disdain the orthodox methods and refuse to provide them, this does not apply.

23. If Ritual Officers, when inscribing talismans, intentionally write upside-down or horizontally, or if red talismans are not written from the left or black talismans not from the right, they shall be sentenced to one year of penal servitude.

24. When Ritual Officers submit petitions for disciples to transmit the Dao and receive ranks, those from the Northern Bureau shall have their ranks calculated by the Northern Bureau, and those from the Southern Bureau by the Southern Bureau. Violators shall be sentenced to eight years of penal servitude.

25. When Ritual Officers submit petitions for disciples to transmit the Dao, they must assess the disciple’s character and integrity before endorsing them. If they recklessly submit petitions due to personal connections or influence, they shall each be sentenced to three years of penal servitude. If the disciple is worthy, the officer shall be promoted by three ranks.

26. When Ritual Officers submit the names of disciples, they must do so on *gengshen* or *jiazi* days. Regardless of the disciple’s current rank, they may be permitted to bear the orthodox methods and submit petitions for promotion to the fourth or fifth rank.

27. If Ritual Officers submit petitions for promotions or irregular advancements on days other than *gengshen* or *jiazi*, they shall each be sentenced to nine years of penal servitude. Repeated petitions shall result in a lifespan reduction of six years.

28. When Ritual Officers transmit the orthodox methods to disciples, they must have personally borne them for over one thousand days before endorsing the disciple. Violators shall be stripped of their ranks and titles, and sentenced to three years of penal servitude.

29. When Ritual Officers transmit the orthodox methods to disciples, they must confer the *Commander’s Seal* and the *General Talisman*, enabling the disciple to command divine officials, deploy ghosts and spirits, and wield the *Commander’s Seal* inscription: *"Seal of the Celestial Capital’s General Command,"* arranged in two lines of stacked seal script.

The *General Talisman* is the *Talisman of the Three Primes*. It is inscribed in seal script on yellow silk. At both ends of the talisman, a black imperial decree is written, granting it to a designated recipient for the command and execution of all gods and spirits. After ordination, the master writes the disciple’s name and affixes their seal; violation of this renders the divine officials unresponsive to the transmitter’s commands, rendering the transmission void. Not only will the ritual be ineffectual, but celestial punishment will surely descend.

1. If Ritual Officers transmit teachings to disciples but fail to complete the instruction, or do not exhaust what should be taught, they shall be sentenced to nine years of penal servitude. If disciples begin learning but do not persist to the end, the same penalty applies. Those who knowingly violate this shall have their sentence increased by one degree. If disciples do not inquire, or masters do not transmit, [the same applies].

2. When Ritual Officers transmit teachings to disciples, regardless of whether they are male or female officials, they must select worthy recipients and may not recklessly divulge the teachings. Violators shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude.

3. If Ritual Officers speak insincerely, with words at odds with their hearts, they shall be sentenced to two years of penal servitude. If circumstances leave no alternative, this does not apply.

4. If Ritual Officers arrogantly exalt themselves and berate their disciples, they shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude.

5. If Ritual Officers encounter extraordinary individuals in the world, they are permitted to submit memorials or petitions. If such a person is implicated in a case, the outer envelope of the memorial shall be inscribed with a vermilion petition, bypassing the celestial bureaus and proceeding directly to the Most High’s presence to await the appointment of an official for promotion, filling a vacancy in the Eighth Rank of the Upper Prime’s immortal offices. If, in daily memorials or petitions, one bypasses the review of the various bureaus, they shall be sentenced to eight years of penal servitude.

6. If Ritual Officers transmit secret methods to disciples but conceal the truth and present falsehoods—whether omitting a single character, reciting the correct incantation but teaching the wrong method, or teaching the correct method but reciting the wrong incantation—they shall be sentenced to six years of penal servitude. Those who knowingly violate this shall have their sentence increased by one degree.

7. Ritual Officers ranked among the Immortal Ministers may memorialize for the appointment of demon-subduing envoys. Violations shall be punished according to the preceding statutes.

8. Ritual Officers ranked among the Lords of the Dao may memorialize for the appointment of officials of the Five Peaks and Four Waterways, as well as replacements for existing immortal or spirit officials in the celestial bureaus. This is permitted.

9. Ritual Officers ranked among the Lofty Perfected may memorialize for the appointment of Lords of the Cavernous Yang and the like. Violations shall be punished according to the preceding statutes.

10. Ritual Officers ranked among the Petition Officers may submit memorials and petitions. Violators shall be sentenced to six years of penal servitude.

11. Ritual Officers who have not received the *Register of Merit Completion* may not submit yellow memorials or present petitions. Violators shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude. Those who knowingly violate this shall be annihilated. Even if unaware, their accumulated transgressions will fill the measure, and the Three Officials shall dispatch agents to seize them, condemning them to suffer for ten thousand kalpas or be cast into the Coal and Stone Hells.

12. Ritual Officers ranked in the Seventh Rank may not address memorials to the Celestial Bureaus or the Five Peaks; only those of the Sixth Rank and above may do so. Violators shall have their lifespan reduced by six years. Those who knowingly violate this shall have their penalty increased by one degree.

13. Ritual Officers ranked among the Southern Bureau and those with official ranks are permitted to submit memorials at their discretion.

14. Ritual Officers ranked among the Northern Bureau and those with official ranks are permitted to transfer to the Southern Bureau for separate consideration by the Most High. Violators shall be stripped of their names and ranks, and their past transgressions shall nullify their merits.

15. Ritual Officers ranked in the Ninth Rank may not address memorials to the Earthly Bureaus; only those of the Eighth Rank and above may do so. Violators shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude.

16. Ritual Officers ranked in the Eighth Rank may memorialize for the appointment of the Left Grand Judgment Officer. Violations shall be punished according to the preceding statutes.

17. Ritual Officers ranked in the Fifth Rank may memorialize for the appointment of Seventh Rank offices. Violations shall be punished according to the preceding statutes.

18. Ritual Officers with official ranks may memorialize for the appointment of Sixth Rank offices. Violations shall be punished according to the preceding statutes.

19. Ritual Officers ranked among the Four Upper Immortals may memorialize for the appointment of Prime Ministers. Violations shall be punished according to the preceding statutes.

20. Ritual Officers ranked among the Perfected may memorialize for the appointment of envoys. Violations shall be punished according to the preceding statutes.

21. Ritual Officers ranked among the Four Ministers may memorialize for the appointment of Immortal Ministers. Violations shall be punished according to the preceding statutes.

22. Ritual Officers ranked among the Envoys of the Nine Heavens may memorialize for the appointment of Immortal Ministers. Violations shall be punished according to the preceding statutes. [Permission to memorialize for Immortal Ministers.]

23. Ritual Officers ranked among the Lords of the Various Bureaus may memorialize for the appointment of Prime Ministers, Perfected Ones, and Judges of Fengdu. Violations shall be punished according to the preceding statutes.

24. If those transmitting the Dao fail to treat their ordination masters with the reverence due a teacher, despite their masters’ pure and sincere devotion, they shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude. Those who deceive or slander their masters or preceptors shall be sentenced to eight years of penal servitude. Those who knowingly violate this shall have their penalty increased by two degrees.

25. If Ritual Officers do not reciprocate the kindness of others but instead demand repayment from their disciples, they shall be sentenced to two years of penal servitude.

26. If Ritual Officers boast of their own merits to others, they shall be sentenced to one year of penal servitude.

27. If disciples recklessly curse their masters or preceptors, their lifespan shall be reduced by six years. Those who knowingly violate this shall have their penalty increased by one degree.

28. If Ritual Officers abuse their authority to humiliate their inferiors or are unfilial to their parents, their lifespan shall be reduced by six years.

29. If Ritual Officers perform talismanic water rituals but do not fully understand the talismanic script, they must seek instruction from a master more accomplished than themselves. If they feign understanding when ignorant or claim mastery when unskilled, arrogantly asserting superiority over others, they shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude.

30. If Ritual Officers submit memorials or petitions but fail to observe proper decorum during audience rites, they shall be censured by the Three Officials. Those of lower rank shall be sentenced to nine years of penal servitude.

31. If Ritual Officers, Daoists, or laypersons conduct rituals for others but ascend the altar with improper attire or demeanor, their lifespan shall be reduced by six years. Those who consume meat or alcohol shall be eternally consigned to Fengdu, and their seven generations of ancestors shall suffer punishment. If they ascend the altar before it is opened, this does not apply.

32. If Ritual Officers or Daoists perform rituals carelessly, submitting memorials or petitions for others without following the proper forms, seeking profit, or lacking sincerity, their lifespan shall be reduced by six years. Those who knowingly violate this shall have their penalty increased by one degree.

33. If Ritual Officers or Daoists enter the altar to present memorials or petitions but use redundant language, fail to observe proper audience decorum, harbor impure thoughts, touch the Gate of Heaven with their hands, or ascend the altar with their right foot first, they shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude.

1. If Ritual Officers or Daoists submit documents stating they present vermilion memorials at a specified time when it is not the proper time, they shall be sentenced to nine years of penal servitude.

2. Ritual Officers or Daoists who receive the *Three Caverns* and *Great Cavern Registers and Scriptures* may command and dispatch all ranks of celestial officials and spirits throughout the world, whether they possess the scriptures without the registers or the registers without the scriptures, the same applies.

3. Ritual Officers or Daoists who ascend to the celestial bureaus to present memorials and petitions or pay homage to the Highest Perfected Ones through secret talismans and incantations must first obtain approval from the relevant offices before proceeding. Those of official rank below the seventh grade are exempt. Those who violate this shall be expelled. Those who proceed without authorization shall be disbarred.

4. If Ritual Officers or Daoists who present memorials and petitions through the secret talismans and incantations of the Most High receive responses but disclose them to others, their lifespan shall be reduced by twelve years.

5. When Ritual Officers or Daoists present memorials and petitions, only those submitted through the Celestial Pivot Institute, the Yellow Memorials of the Mysterious Capital, or the headers of memorials and petitions for ritual offerings may not contain a single erroneous character. Violations shall render the ritual void, and the memorials shall fail to reach the officiating Ritual Officer. Both the Ritual Officer and the petitioner shall be sentenced to five years of penal servitude.

6. If Ritual Officers or Daoists list incorrect ranks or falsely claim official titles, their lifespan shall be reduced by six years.

7. Only Ritual Officers, Daoists, or laypersons who have received the *Orthodox Unity Register* may submit official documents or present yellow memorials. Violators shall be sentenced to nine years of penal servitude.

8. If Ritual Officers or Daoists present memorials and petitions without using the five-layered sealing method, they shall be sentenced to four years of penal servitude.

9. If Ritual Officers or Daoists submit documents where they ought not to use the term "your subject" but do so, their lifespan shall be reduced by six years. Those who knowingly violate this shall have their penalty increased by one degree.

10. If Ritual Officers or Daoists submit documents where they ought to use the term "your subject" but do not, they shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude. Those who knowingly violate this shall be sentenced to nine years of penal servitude.

11. Memorials and petitions for ritual offerings presented by Ritual Officers or Daoists may not exceed one hundred seventy-six characters. Violators shall each be sentenced to four years of penal servitude. The same applies to those who allow errors in the ritual text. Those who knowingly violate this shall have their penalty increased by one degree.

12. When Ritual Officers or Daoists present memorials and petitions for others, they must do so according to proper form. For other ritual offerings, they may perform services according to their capacity. If the petitioner is poor or of low status and unable to afford more, even a single lamp or flower suffices. However, those who lack sincerity shall be sentenced to five years of penal servitude.

13. If Ritual Officers or Daoists conduct rituals and ascend the altar without reverence, engage in frivolous speech, laughter, or clamor, cast sidelong glances, stand with their backs turned, spit, summon servants, or fail to observe the decorum of a subject, their lifespan shall be reduced by six years. Those who knowingly violate this shall have their penalty increased by one degree.

14. If Ritual Officers or Daoists present memorials and petitions that do not conform to the proper format, they shall be sentenced to two years of penal servitude. For every two additional errors, the penalty shall increase by one degree. If two errors occur, the memorials shall not reach the High Priest or the ritual sponsor, and both shall be sentenced to nine years of penal servitude.

15. When Ritual Officers or Daoists present memorials for salvation and deliverance, they may only fill eighty *Golden Register* and *White Simpl* sets, or eighty *Yellow Register* sets for offering rituals. For memorials of eighty-one sections, only eight hundred sets may be offered. Exceeding these numbers shall render the memorials unusable, and both the Ritual Officer and the ritual sponsor shall be sentenced to nine years of penal servitude. Those who knowingly violate this shall have their penalty increased by one degree.

16. Ritual Officers or Daoists below the fifth rank who have not received the *Capital Merit Register* may not submit documents to the *Nüqing* or issue summons to souls. Violators shall have their lifespan reduced by six years. Those who knowingly violate this shall have their penalty increased by one degree.

17. Ritual Officers of the fifth rank or above who issue summons must include the following in their vermilion documents: *"Having reverently observed the Crimson Writ of the Primal Chaos and the Nüqing Summons, permission is granted to proclaim this throughout the boundless worlds and the courts of the ritual officiants, to deliver the deceased to the Celestial Palaces and advance them into the Daoist abodes, in accordance with the Most High’s compassion for all beings."* If this language is omitted, the summons shall not be effective in the netherworld, and both the Ritual Officer and the ritual sponsor shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude.

18. Offering tablets must be nine inches in length, two *fen* in thickness, and two inches and four *fen* in width. The front shall bear the incantation, and the back shall be inscribed with talismanic script. They shall be wrapped in blue silk or gauze; if resources are insufficient, paper may be used. Violations of this format shall render the tablets unusable in the Southern Chang Prefecture of the netherworld, and the ritual shall be void. Both the Ritual Officer and the ritual sponsor shall be sentenced to two years of penal servitude.

19. When laypersons conduct rituals, they may not burn sandalwood, benzoin, or frankincense. Only *hundred-harmonies incense* may be used, as this invites the descent and witness of the Highest Perfected Ones. Those with means may burn *descending-perfected incense*. Violators shall bring retribution upon three generations of their family, while the Ritual Officers or Daoists involved shall have their lifespan reduced by three years.

20. If laypersons slander the Orthodox Dao and turn from the true to the heterodox, five generations of their family shall be punished, and they themselves shall encounter misfortune in all affairs. Their parents, spouse, and children shall suffer the consequences, and after death, they shall be sent to Fengdu, never to be released for ten thousand kalpas.

21. If laypersons mistake the true for the false and the false for the true, and slander Ritual Officers, after death they shall be sent to Fengdu for punishment. Seven generations of their ancestors and nine generations of their forebears shall suffer calamity, and they themselves shall encounter many misfortunes in life. Those who wear the talismanic cord shall have their penalty increased by two degrees; those who commit severe offenses shall have their lifespan reduced according to the gravity of their crimes.

22. If laypersons abuse their power to damage Daoist abbeys, destroy scriptures, slander Daoists, or oppress the innocent, their lifespan shall be reduced. In severe cases, they shall be annihilated, and nine generations of their ancestors shall suffer calamity.

23. If laypersons act cunningly to harm others, nine generations of their ancestors shall suffer calamity. After death, they shall sink into the netherworld, never to be released for ten thousand kalpas. Ritual Officers or Daoists who witness such acts shall have their own lives forfeit.

24. If laypersons utter foul or vulgar language in Daoist abbeys, sacred mountains, or places where scriptures and talismans are kept, they shall be sentenced to one year of penal servitude. Ritual Officers or Daoists who do the same shall have their penalty increased by one degree.

25. If laypersons presumptuously imitate Ritual Officers by pacing the stars, they shall be sentenced to two years of penal servitude. Those who tread upon the stars of the Perfected Ones shall have their lifespan reduced by three years.

The penalty for Ritual Officers or Daoists shall be increased by one degree.

26. If laypersons curse heaven and earth, the sun and moon, expose themselves to the Three Luminaries, rebuke the wind or point at the rain, or resent and blame heaven and earth, they shall be sentenced to nine years of penal servitude. The penalty for Ritual Officers or Daoists shall be increased by one degree.

27. If laypersons burn incense with fire from the stove, urinate in its presence, or are seen by those observing the precepts, they shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude. The penalty for Ritual Officers or Daoists shall be increased by one degree.

28. If laypersons consume meat or alcohol and ascend the Three Clarities Hall or face the Supreme Perfected Ones, they shall be sentenced to six years of penal servitude. Those who knowingly violate this shall have their penalty increased by one degree. The penalty for Ritual Officers or Daoists shall be increased by two degrees.

29. If laypersons recklessly sacrifice to licentious ghosts or deities not recorded in the sacrificial canons, they shall be sentenced to two years of penal servitude.

30. If laypersons, in their suffering, seek talismanic water without sincerity, they shall be sentenced to two years of penal servitude.

31. If laypersons hold fasts or establish jiao rites without sincerity, they shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude.

32. If laypersons bring scriptures, texts, secret formulas, or talismans into defiled or unclean places, they shall be sentenced to two years of penal servitude. The penalty for Ritual Officers or Daoists shall be increased by one degree.

33. If laypersons transmit or receive secret methods without submitting their names, or secretly view sacred texts, they shall be sentenced to three years of penal servitude.

34. If laypersons do not practice orthodox methods or secretly keep secret methods in their homes, they shall be sentenced to two years of penal servitude.

35. If laypersons resent the sovereign, are unfilial to their elders, unkind to their siblings, untrustworthy to their teachers and friends, unharmonious with their spouses, or instead of aiding the poor, despise and humiliate them, they shall be sentenced to two years of penal servitude. In severe cases, they shall forfeit their lives.

**Regulations and Ritual Norms Chapter**
The Supreme Pole Master of the Dao says: The regulations for fasts and jiao rites are of utmost gravity. All these prohibitions are the ancient statutes of the Mysterious Capital’s Upper Palace, meant to admonish negligence, correct transgressions, examine conduct, and observe sincerity. If there are faults, they shall be rectified according to the matter at hand. This is not to punish those who partake in fasts but to open the door for correcting faults. If one knows their errors and reforms, their offenses shall also be absolved. Thus, the great discipline of the Dao may be upheld, its profound transformation spread, and those who ascend the altar should take heed and be warned.

**Titles of Jiao Altar Offices**

**High Merit (Gaogong)** – Also called Left Expounder of the Dao
His duty: His virtue is inwardly full, his dignity outwardly complete. Heaven and humanity turn toward him; ghosts and spirits gaze upon him. He treads the luminous steps and flies the memorials, receiving and proclaiming virtue, bestowing grace upon the Three Realms, his ritual surpassing all others.

**Capital Lecturer (Dujang)** – Also called Right Expounder of the Dao
His duty: His virtue is profoundly supportive, his mastery of the law refined. He assists in chanting the ritual norms, leading the ranks in order. On the sacred altar, he paces and ascends the seat to expound, his brilliance matching the people’s expectations, silently resonating with their hearts.

**Fast Supervisor (Jianzhai)** – Also called Left Assistant of the Teaching
His duty: He holds the statutes, oversees the regulations and prohibitions, corrects the altar officers, and upholds solemn dignity. He meticulously examines faults, calmly receives the writs, maintaining strictness and support, neither lax nor excessive.

**Scripture Attendant (Shijing)** – Also called Right Assistant of the Teaching
His duty: He keeps the tables and cases immaculate, arranges the scrolls in order. He opens the cases to present memorials, stores the caskets and displays the texts, harmonizes the many voices, and serves as a model for all duties. His presence brings harmony to all, uniting the hidden and manifest in joy.

**Incense Attendant (Shixiang)** – Also called Left Supporter of the Celestial Court
His duty: He meticulously adorns the ritual vessels, purifies the tables and mats. He tends the incense burners, ensuring fragrance from beginning to end. The mysterious mirror shines clearly, his cinnabar sincerity is revealed, never allowing interruption, ever preserving his initial devotion.

**Lamp Attendant (Shideng)** – Also called Right Supporter of the Celestial Court
His duty: He prepares the lanterns and censers, keeps the lamps and candles immaculate. Their light shines high and low, illuminating inside and out. Through the night, the glow continues; at dawn, the flames are adorned. Above, they brighten the Dao’s realm; below, they illuminate the netherworld.

**Refiner (Lianshi)**
His duty: His integrity is pure within and without, his heart like the Great Void. His virtue embodies compassion, his sincerity extends mercy. He achieves the wondrous use of Kan and Li, uniting with the primordial work of creation. Refining his form to merge with the Perfected, he transcends the ordinary to enter sainthood.

**Ritual Overseer (Sheke)**
His duty: He upholds solemn dignity, proclaims the mysterious norms. He approaches the tables with reverence, drawing near the Dao. His voice carries the resonance of gold and jade, his responses chime like jade chimes. He must be respectful and cautious, offering gratitude and prayers.

**Ritual Director (Zhengyi)**
His duty: He is thoroughly versed in ritual norms, strictly upholding the mysterious principles. He must be a seasoned and mature individual, embodying the standards of dignity and rectitude. If the High Merit falters in his duties, the Ritual Director particularly assists and corrects. Should there be any lapses, he must investigate and verify.

**Altar Supervisor (Jiantan)**
His duty: He eliminates impurity and promotes purity, subdues evil and assists the upright. In ascending the altar and serving, he must first ensure strict cleanliness. To communicate with the Perfected and reach the numinous, he must rely on the power of supervision. He must be reverent and meticulous, never allowing delays or errors.

**Path Purifier (Qingdao)**
His duty: He purifies the cloud paths, sweeps away dust and defilement. He repels demonic influences from the Yellow Road, drives away malevolent spirits from the immortal ranks. He must not lag behind, leading the way at the appointed time.

**Censer Keeper (Zhilu)** – Also called Left Director of Ritual
His duty: He upholds the dignity of the numinous teaching, leading the immortal ranks. In conducting rituals with composure, his sincerity must be steadfast and dignified. In maintaining strict discipline, he must not deviate from the proper order. His rituals should be thorough, his affairs without discrepancy.

**Bell Keeper (Zhiqing)** – Also called Right Director of Ritual
His duty: He chants the cavernous hymns, extols the mysterious norms. He harmonizes the qi and the atmosphere, his steps through the void resounding to the clouds. With unified body and mind, his refined and elegant sounds transmit through the altar’s sacred space. He propagates the supreme Dao, uniting with the Perfected and the numinous.

**Petition Reciter (Cichan)** – Also called Left Registrar of the Straight
His duty: He must uphold steadfast and upright thoughts, facing the Celestial Sovereign. He communicates sincerity to the upper heavens, confessing faults and seeking blessings for the people below. His demeanor must be solemn and grave, never negligent or careless.

**Announcer (Biaobai)** – Also called Right Registrar of the Straight
His duty: He presents offerings with reverence, focusing his thoughts with precision. He should exhaust his light in one devotion, that his sincerity may reach the Three Realms. His dignity and caution must be observed, and the ritual norms followed.

**Ritual Regulations for the Jiao Altar**

For those who commit breaches of decorum upon ascending the altar, the regulations provide precedent: they shall be penalized with offerings of incense and oil to atone for their transgressions, or, in lieu thereof, with prostrations as recompense.

All who ascend the altar must each bring their own ritual sash, robe, bamboo tablet, and shoes; these may not be exchanged with others at the last moment. Should any public duty be neglected, one may not appropriate garments not one’s own.

In all matters of ascent, movement, or repose upon the altar, one must first report to the Dharma Master, bow once in reverence before the incense, then depart after three bows. The same procedure applies upon re-entry.

Should one ascend the altar with disordered footwear, the penalty is twenty prostrations. For sitting or rising without proper order, five prostrations. For leaning or sitting improperly, five prostrations. For engaging in conversation with outsiders while chanting or assisting in ritual affairs, twenty prostrations. For overturning incense or fire, ten prostrations. For discussing worldly affairs, twenty prostrations. For frivolous speech or laughter, ten prostrations. For exchanging ornate or improper words, five prostrations. For spilling lamp oil, fifty prostrations. For disheveled sash or robe, ten prostrations. For the *Dujang* (Chief Cantor) failing in chant or decorum, twenty prostrations. For the *Zhijiu* (Disciplinarian) who, upon witnessing faults, conceals private misdeeds, sixty prostrations. For inattentive scripture attendants, or those who disturb the high seat or neglect ritual objects, ten prostrations. For the incense attendant whose incense smoke is interrupted, forty prostrations. For the lamp attendant whose flame is extinguished, twenty prostrations. For the Jiao Master who fails to maintain discipline within or without, or whose voice is raised in harshness, fifty prostrations. For those listening to scripture who lean or recline without holding their tablets, ten prostrations. For those who fail to focus their thoughts on purity and emptiness, allowing their minds to wander or their spirits to flag—if detected by the assembly—thirty prostrations. For entering or exiting the Jiao altar without reporting to the *Jianzhai* (Fast Supervisor), twenty prostrations. For loose hair or hurried steps, two prostrations. For reciting scripture with errors or disrupting the text with improper questions, thirty prostrations. For discordant chanting, twenty prostrations. For ascending the altar without washing hands or rinsing the mouth, twenty prostrations. For improper bows or失礼 in sitting and rising, five prostrations. For circumambulating out of order or moving against the flow, ten prostrations. For rising, walking, or returning to one’s seat without paying homage to the scriptures, three prostrations. For departing abruptly, twenty prostrations. For rushing forward while offering incense, ten prostrations. For the Jiao Master’s negligence in preparing offerings, twenty prostrations. For failing to report received instructions to superiors, thirty prostrations. For leaving the altar without authorization during the ritual, one hundred prostrations. Those who refuse to accept discipline for breaches of decorum shall be expelled from the altar and barred from further participation.

Should any of the assembled officials fail in their ritual duties, they shall be disciplined according to the severity of the offense: from one to three infractions, penalties shall be imposed as prescribed. For three or more infractions, they shall be removed from the fast and expelled. Should the *Zhijiu* (Disciplinarian) fail to report such faults, they shall share the same penalty.

Should the presiding Dharma Master err in ritual matters, they shall acknowledge their fault and submit a report to the *Jianzhai* (Fast Supervisor). For one to three infractions, penalties shall be imposed as prescribed. For three or more infractions, their merit shall be suspended for three hundred days, and they shall not be permitted to occupy the Dharma seat again.

**Titles and Offices in Daoist Abbeys and Observatories (Ancient System)**
- *Zhenren* (Perfected)
- *Jijiu* (Libationer)
- *Daolu* (Dao Registrar)
- *Daoji* (Dao Chronicler)
- *Daotong* (Dao Lineage Keeper)
- *Daozheng* (Dao Rectifier)
- *Daohui* (Dao Assembly Head)
- *Guanzhu* (Abbot)
- *Tidian* (Supervisor)
- *Tiju* (Director)
- *Fugong* (Vice Abbey Head)
- *Fuguan* (Vice Abbot)
- *Jiangshi* (Lecturer)
- *Dujian* (General Supervisor)
- *Xuanyi* (Mystical Exegesis Master)
- *Zhishi* (Manager)
- *Shuji* (Scribe)
- *Jianyi* (Remonstrator)
- *Jianlin* (Overseer)
- *Jieshi* (Preceptor)
- *Shangzuo* (Senior Seat)
- *Zhangji* (Registrar)
- *Dubiao* (Chief Usher)
- *Jianzhai* (Fast Supervisor)
- *Fashi* (Dharma Master)
- *Fubiao* (Deputy Usher)
- *Zhensui* (True Year Officer)
- *Biaobai* (Announcer)
- *Zhiku* (Treasurer)
- *Silunsha* (Silk and Sandal Officer)

**Newly Added Offices at the Southern Culmen Longevity Abbey (Fifteen Positions)**
- *Zangshi* (Archivist): Oversees scriptures and records in the repository hall.
- *Zhiyuan* (Dean): Manages all affairs of the Daoist academy.
- *Shiyuan* (Refectory Head): Same as above.
- *Zhuhan* (Chancellor): Specializes in composing texts and instructing novices in reading.
- *Xunshi* (Preceptor): Teaches novices the fast rituals, precepts, and doctrines.
- *Siji* (Bursar): Manages accounts, grain registers, and all documents.
- *Zhidian* (Hall Steward): Oversees halls, corridors, and all buildings.
- *Zhitang* (Refectory Steward): Manages matters of the refectory and meals.
- *Zhibin* (Guest Master): Receives and attends to visiting Daoist companions.
- *Silin* (Granary Keeper): Manages all grain storage.
- *Sihu* (Gatekeeper): Oversees entry and exit at the gates.
- *Sigong* (Works Officer): Manages construction and repairs.
- *Sipu* (Garden Steward): Oversees medicinal gardens, vegetable plots, and orchards.
- *Sisheng* (Livestock Officer): Manages sacrificial animals and livestock.
- *Shezhu* (Estate Steward): Oversees farmlands, spring planting, and autumn harvest.

**Regulations for Abbeys and Observatories**
Prohibited: Discussing the Dao to convert the wise or propagating Buddhism to delude the foolish.

Emperor Cheng of Jin inquired during the Xianhe era: In Leimai and other commanderies, eighty scholars of Buddhism petitioned to enter temples as monks, while in Mount Song and other places, two hundred Daoists petitioned to have their names expunged and enter the Dao. The Emperor said to Wang Dao: "I have often said that the human body is hard to obtain, and birth in the Central Land is difficult to attain. Yet now the people of the Central Land turn away from the Dao of the Central Land, wishing to be reborn in the West as Hu barbarians rather than live in the Central Land—how utterly foolish! Is this not what Emperor Zhang of Han meant by 'the Dao transforms the wise, while Buddhism transforms the foolish'?"

Wang Dao replied: "Truly, these are words of truth, worthy to serve as a warning for ten thousand generations." Thus, the saying "the Dao transforms the wise, while Buddhism transforms the foolish" arose from this time. Though such words have since become common, Daoists must never utter them, lest they invite calamity—this must be strictly avoided.

According to the Perfected One Huangfu: "The sages established teachings in accordance with local customs and natural dispositions. Those whose endowment is dull, rooted in earth, find joy in death; those whose nature is keen, rooted in heaven, find joy in life. As the *Book of Changes* says: 'That which is rooted in heaven inclines upward; that which is rooted in earth inclines downward.' Those who find joy in death—did not the Buddha in the *Nirvana Sutra* speak of 'sitting in extinction, extinction itself being bliss'? Those who find joy in life—did not Laozi say, 'My fate lies with me, not with Heaven'? The Way of life and death exists for both the wise and the foolish; why then must we insist on distinguishing their joys?"

This passage expounds the matter most reasonably, and so it is adopted here.

**Prohibition on Disputation**
A great taboo is to engage in disputes with others. The teaching of the *Daodejing* states: "Only by not contending does the world find none able to contend with him." The *Qingzheng Jing* says: "The superior person does not contend; the inferior person delights in contention." Should one encounter hostility or slander in speech, one should respond thus: "I have often said that the human body is hard to obtain, and birth in the Central Land is difficult to attain. Having been born in the Central Land, one rarely encounters the true Dao. I am a person of the Central Land, born in the Central Land, and I follow the Dao of the sages of the Central Land. This is to neither betray the Heaven that gave me life nor the earth that bore me. For a person of the Central Land to follow the Dao of the Central Land is only right. To turn one’s back on the land of one’s birth and follow another teaching is to be no better than a beast, forgetting the virtue of Heaven and Earth that gave one life—how can such a one be called a cultivator? As for the superiority or inferiority of teachings, I know not. But this answer alone places principle above personal bias."

**Seating Order for Daoists and Buddhists**
Whenever Buddhists visit an abbey or observatory, Daoists should yield the higher seat to them. When Daoists visit a temple, Buddhists should likewise yield the higher seat to them. This is the courtesy due to visiting guests. If they meet elsewhere, both Daoists and Buddhists should defer to the elder, yielding the higher seat. Though their teachings differ as between Chinese and barbarian, harmony is the highest principle, and humility is the virtue of the cultivated.

**Designations of Monks and Daoists**
In the fifth year of Yonghui during the reign of Emperor Gaozong of Tang, a Hu monk and a Daoist disputed over their titles, saying: "One only hears people call us 'monk-Daoist,' never 'Daoist-monk.' Why should the Hu monk be ranked above the Daoist?" The Emperor said: "The Daoist is a person of Our Central Land, while you are a Hu barbarian. How can a man of the barbarian tribes be placed above a person of the Central Land? In the rites of the Central Land, the honored are distinguished from the lowly. Barbarians are not even permitted to wear caps or shoes in the same manner as the Chinese—how much less should they be ranked above them!"

The Emperor said to Yu Zhining: "Consider the common saying 'barbarian and Chinese.' The Chinese are the Central Land, yet they are placed below the barbarians—is this right? Or take the terms 'yin and yang': yin is the petty man, inauspicious; yang is the noble man, auspicious. Should yang be placed below yin? Or take 'fortune and misfortune': why not speak of fortune first and misfortune after? Is misfortune to be considered auspicious? Or take 'ghosts and spirits': ghosts are lowly spirits, while spirits are celestial. Why should lowly ghosts be placed above celestial spirits? Or take 'crooked and straight, slanted and upright': upright and straight are good, while slanted and crooked are not. Should the inauspicious names come first? In all common sayings, the inauspicious and unpropitious are placed first. The ranking of monks and Daoists, high and low, follows the same principle. From this, one may discern the order of auspicious and inauspicious, noble and base, Chinese and barbarian. By this example, how can the dignity of the Dao be sullied?"

**Prohibition on Discussing Karma and Retribution**
Emperor Wuzong of Tang said: "In the Tianjian era of Emperor Wu of Liang, the name 'Righteous Students' was established. Each was required to fabricate ten tales of hellish karma and retribution to delude the world, bewitching the foolish to seek alms. Over eight hundred such tales were compiled and scattered throughout barbarian books like the *Dharma Garden* and *Forest of Gems*. The original transmission of the Buddhist teachings had the patriarchs going barefoot, carrying alms bowls, and begging for food as their practice—how could deluding the world to seek wealth be considered cultivation? Yet the foolish men and women of the world are all deceived, some even squandering their fortunes to make offerings. The Dao of the Daoist household is the Dao of the sages of the Central Land. One must never speak of ghosts, demons, strange omens, hellish karma, or retribution, nor beg in the streets to shame the ancestral teachings. Such conduct is that of shameless vagrants, not of the people of the Central Land. It must be strictly prohibited."

**Prohibition on False Relics**
There was a Daoist named Tao Yuansu who met another Daoist claiming to be Huangfu Xin. He led Tao to a pine tree, where they saw a white-bearded man who felt Tao’s head and said: "This child has extraordinary bones in his skull. One day, he may study the Dao."

The parents then offered him to the Temple of Supreme Heaven, where he became a disciple of the Daoist Teng Bixiao. At nineteen, he encountered an elder who bestowed upon him the *Book of Celestial Marrow and Numinous Scripture*. Later, he wandered to Mount Kongtong and subsequently resided on Mount Taihua. By the thirteenth year of the Tianjian reign of the Liang Dynasty (the year jiawu), he was one hundred and eighty-two years old. The Emperor, having heard of his transcendent and ethereal Dao, dispatched envoys with an imperial edict to summon him, but he declined. He then composed a poem:

*"Through Jin, Qi, and Liang—nearly two centuries passed,
Yet worldly affairs still kept me apart.
Today, I shed this mortal coil,
And ascend to the Great Veil beside the Heavenly Emperor."*

Thereupon, he instructed his disciples: "I shall now depart. Cremate my body and do not preserve my remains." He then removed his robe, sat cross-legged, and passed away. This occurred on the first day of the first month in the fourteenth year of the Tianjian reign (the year yiwei). His disciples cremated him, and among his bones were found pearls of red and white, some as large as soybeans, radiant and lustrous, as hard as stone—three hundred and ninety-nine in total. The envoys presented them to the court.

Fan Yun remarked: "The Western Hu possess secret arts of the bedchamber, wherein they absorb yin through the union of qi, producing objects of red and purple hues, heavy in weight. The Hu call these *śarīra*. The Western Hu also have a hard stone indistinguishable from these, often using *dragon’s blood resin* to dye bones, which deceivers in China, skilled in self-cultivation, claim to be formed from the pure yang essence of virgin youths—red and white in color, calling them *yang essence* or *śarīra*. Yet these are nothing but the foul remnants of the dead. How could they be presented to the court? If one has attained the Dao, they must ascend to heaven, leaving no withered bones behind. If death occurs, and the corpse is burned, the foul remnants remain—how can this be called attainment? Among common households, even a single dead man’s bones left in a dwelling are called *hidden corpses*, inauspicious objects that must be exorcised by shamans. How much worse to bring them into the court! The envoys should be executed."

The Emperor thus refused the offering. Thereupon, the Celestial Master issued a decree to all temples and abbeys across the land: "Since the time of the Yellow Emperor, the sages of the Central Land have never regarded death as attainment of the Dao. If one claims to have attained the Dao, why would they still die? They would merely be called lesser ghosts. Moreover, to leave behind the remains of a corpse, forming so-called *yang essence*, and deceive the foolish masses—henceforth, anyone who dares to delude others with *yang essence* or *śarīra* shall be condemned as a heretic."

**Admonitions on Ritual Burning and Cultivation**
According to the principles of ritual burning and cultivation: The duty of the Daoist is to burn incense and light lamps morning and evening, offer water and flowers, recite scriptures, and perform repentance rites to seek blessings and merit. The halls must be kept immaculate, with sacred images and ritual implements spotless and orderly, not a speck of dust allowed to settle. They must be constantly dusted and swept, ensuring no filth or grime accumulates on the floors or within the halls and chambers. This is called laboring with devotion before the Heavenly Emperor, entrusting oneself to a realm of purity and nobility—the true duty of a Daoist. Those who uphold this will surely receive blessings and rewards. To neglect even one of these duties is to invite calamity from heaven.

Moreover, one must devote all effort to tending vegetable gardens and exhaust all care in cooking and hearth-tending, gathering firewood and drawing water, enduring hardship in body and mind, toiling with limbs and flesh—this is called accumulating the merit of diligent labor, by which one repays the grace of Heaven and Earth for the gift of life. If one’s merit is great, the blessings and rewards shall be profound. Should one fail in both these duties, one bears the name of Daoist in vain, secretly enjoying the blessings of heaven and humanity. After the body perishes, one shall receive the retribution of an animal’s fate, escaping neither the suffering of the butcher’s block nor the torment of punishment—how can one not fear this!

Here is appended the *Admonitions Verified* by Cao Jinwei of Tanzhou. Its summary states:

Formerly, Kou Qianzhi said: All who are born into the world under the baleful influence of inauspicious stars, destined for poverty and early death, owe this to the deep-rooted karmic offenses accumulated over past lives. They ought to renounce the household life, serve the Heavenly Emperor, and seek the power of blessings—only then may they escape poverty and early death. Thus did the practice of ritual burning and renunciation flourish.

In former times, there was one Xu Mi of Jingzhao, who from childhood suffered many illnesses. A diviner declared: “This child will not only be plagued by illness but is also fated for poverty and early death.” His parents did not yet believe it. When he reached eleven, a physiognomist said: “This child’s lifespan will not exceed fifteen years.” His parents, startled, asked: “Is there a way to avert this?” The physiognomist replied: “If you wish to escape calamity, there is no recourse but to leave the household life—only then may he barely survive.”

Thereupon, he was given to the Xuanyuan Abbey east of the city to become a Daoist. Mi diligently tended the incense and lamps morning and evening, sweeping the halls and corridors. Broom in hand, he did not shrink from labor in the winding cloisters; sickle and hoe in tow, he constantly exerted himself in the garden plots. At night, he recited scriptures and performed repentance rites, sat in meditation with focused intent, burned incense, and bowed in earnest devotion. Daily, he repaired walls and mended roofs, never idle for a moment. His hands and feet grew calloused and cracked like tortoise shells. Moreover, he did not consume the abbey’s provisions but prepared his own daily meals; he did not burn the abbey’s firewood but gathered his own and drew his own water for sustenance.

Someone, pitying his toil, asked: “Why do you endure so much hardship?” He replied: “For one who cultivates the Dao, the matter of life and death is grave—how can I rely on others to serve me while I do nothing myself? If others become my servants, I shall instead receive the blessings of heaven and humanity.”

Thus he persevered for over forty years. One day, he encountered a Daoist beneath the shade of a pine, who took his hand and said: “You have labored long. The Heavenly Emperor grants you longevity and bestows upon you rank. Soon, your name shall resound throughout the capital.” The court later appointed him Director of Daoist Affairs at Jingxiao Abbey, conferring upon him the title *Perfected One Who Assists the Dao and Transforms the World*. In the first year of Emperor Fei of the Southern Qi, during the reign of Yulin Wang, he reached the age of one hundred and one. The ruler of Northern Wei, hearing of this, dispatched envoys bearing gold and silk.

As his Dao neared completion, he met three Daoists who invited him to wander with them. He went, and during the reign of Emperor Yuan of Liang, there was one who abandoned Buddhism to study the Dao. Entering Mount Mao, he encountered Mi, who rode a great ram and said: “Go and tell the Daoist community of Xuanyuan Abbey in Jingzhao: I now serve as Left Minister of the Heavenly Canopy’s Inner Terrace in the Celestial Bureau.” From then on, his story spread throughout the world, and all who renounced the household life to serve the Dao came to know the value of diligence and reverence. Alas!

When those who leave the household life first do so, it is often because their fate is afflicted by inauspicious stars, and their parents, fearing they cannot be raised, relinquish their six kin to entrust them to abbeys and temples. Later generations, unaware of cause and effect, have seen those who leave the household become mostly indolent, arrogant, and dissolute. They eat others’ food to sate their hunger, accept others’ reverence, seek others’ alms, yet feel no momentary shame. They pay no heed to whether incense burns in the halls, whether the steps and courtyards are clean, or whether the temple walls and roofs are crumbling. Idly they enjoy ease and pleasure, refusing to labor in devotion or reverence. Compared to the example of Xu Mi, how can this not weigh upon their hearts?

Moreover, the laws of Heaven are stern, and the eyes of spirits see all. Though one may briefly enjoy warmth and fullness, one may yet fall into a lower existence! How could one advance toward the trials of Fengdu? From the age of eight, I left the household life, and now my hair has turned white. Often, as I face my meal, I reflect in silence, having done nothing to aid the teachings. I fear my transgressions run deep, and calamity may befall my nine generations. Thus, I universally admonish those who have left the household: all should awaken sharply, lest they sink into torpor. Perhaps then we may together ascend the ladder of immortality and uphold the Mysterious Principle.

In the second year of the Jianlong era of the Great Song, on the day following the full moon of the third month, Bai Jinwei, a Daoist of Shouxing Abbey in Tanzhou, Successor of the Orthodox Teaching of the Three Caverns, Subduer of Demons in the Qingwei Tradition, respectfully writes this.

**Morning and Evening Observances**
All who dwell in jade halls and numinous abodes must prioritize the burning of incense and the performance of rites. The abbot of each abbey or temple shall daily gather the community to ascend the hall, burn incense, chant scriptures, pay homage to the Perfected, and offer veneration to the Sages. They should embody the heart of serving Heaven and upholding the teachings, thereby fulfilling their resolve to cultivate the Dao and refine their virtue.

**New and Full Moons**
On the days of the new and full moons, bathe the day prior. At dawn on the appointed day, the abbot shall lead the community, clad in Daoist robes, to ascend the hall in proper order and chant scriptures—first to extend the Sage’s longevity, and second to propagate the Mysterious Model.

**Festivals and Seasonal Observances**
On festival and seasonal days, perform the rites of homage to the Perfected. After the morning observances, revive the customs of antiquity by gathering in ritual feasts, strictly adhering to ancestral norms, and avoiding neglect or dissipation.

**Opening and Closing the Abbey Gates**
The gates of abbeys and temples are of utmost importance and must be opened and closed at set times. At dawn, wait for light to open them; at dusk, close them while there is still light. The locks shall be managed by the designated officer, while the keys shall be kept by the common treasury. Opening and closing must follow the appointed times.

**Morning Bells and Evening Drums**
The morning bell and evening drum summon the hundred spirits, enhance the majesty of abbeys and temples, and amplify the grandeur of mountains and mausoleums. They must not be omitted at dawn or dusk.

**Morning Tea Assembly**
After ascending the hall each day, Daoists and novices shall gather in the abbot’s quarters, arrange themselves in ranks, and bow before the abbot. Then, dividing into two ranks, the community shall bow to one another. Daoists shall sit in order, while novices shall bow toward the abbot, serve tea in turn, and stand respectfully below. The abbot shall address the day’s affairs. If there are public or private matters, official duties, or urgent business, they shall be reported to the abbot and discussed openly before the community. Once concluded, all shall bow and withdraw, upholding the pure regulations and not neglecting the Mysterious Teachings.

**The Common Treasury**
The common treasury is the guiding principle of abbeys and temples. Those appointed to manage it must be persons of great virtue, magnanimity, impartiality, and integrity. All matters—such as the receipt and disbursement of money, grain, and cloth; the cultivation of fields, gardens, and mountain lands; and the reception of visiting officials—shall be assigned to various officers, who shall take turns overseeing them for one, two, or three years. They must act with fairness, devote themselves to the public good, and never favor private interests.

The Perfected Lord Yuchan said: “The common treasury’s wealth and grain are overseen by spirits; those who take from it shall each receive their karmic retribution.” These words are not empty—one must be vigilant and cautious.

**Refectory and Banquets**

**Attending Refectory Banquets**

When attending banquets in the refectory, one must take one’s seat in proper order, strictly observing the rites of seniority and precedence—no disorder shall be permitted.

**Daily Meals in the Refectory**
Each person shall be allotted one *sheng* of rice per day from the common treasury. Vegetables, fish, and meat shall be provided according to abundance or scarcity. The three daily meals shall be announced by striking the cloud-shaped board, summoning the assembly to partake at the appointed times.

When itinerant Daoist travelers arrive, they shall be provided meals for seven days in accordance with ancient custom. Those who remain through winter or summer to sit in meditation shall be furnished meals for one hundred days. Daoists serving in an official capacity at the abbey or temple shall receive rice on the same terms as resident Daoists and are not subject to these limitations.

When eminent and virtuous masters from the capital or renowned sacred mountains visit the abbey or temple, they shall be received with doubled courtesy, their needs supplied without regard to duration.

**Refectory Stores**
Daily provisions shall follow established regulations. No private favors shall be granted, nor shall supplies be arbitrarily disbursed for personal use. Should vulgar and shameless individuals demand wine or food, those managing the common treasury must uphold the regulations with strict discipline. Violators shall be penalized by sponsoring a vegetarian feast at their own expense, to which the entire abbey must be invited.

**Taking Disciples**
In accepting disciples, one must select the descendants of reputable families with long-standing virtue and renown in their communities—those who have cultivated the Dao and accumulated merit. They must be free of debilitating illnesses or impediments. Only those received with proper rites and bound as Daoist kin may be admitted.

The descendants of indolent or worldly artisans, as well as those afflicted with foul odors, blindness, facial scars, polydactyly, lameness, or other disabilities, shall not be taken as disciples.

**Issuing Ordination Certificates**
When Daoist novices enter the Mystic Gate in childhood and study the scriptures of their tradition, those who mature into individuals of true Daoist capacity may, upon reaching the appropriate year for ordination, submit a petition to the Daoist Registry in advance to ensure a smooth process. Thus, the transmission shall endure from generation to generation without interruption.

**Ritual Offerings**
When patrons bring incense to the abbey or temple to sponsor ritual offerings, the common treasury shall assign responsible Daoists to oversee all related matters. They must perform their duties with reverence, adhering to the rites of celestial worship, and never betray the patron’s trust.

**Soliciting Alms**
When abbeys or temples undertake renovations, they must rely on the generosity of benefactors. The common treasury shall appoint capable individuals to carry petitions and solicit funds and grain to support the construction.

First, they should seek out wealthy and virtuous households in distant regions known for their benevolence. If such families are genuinely inclined to donate, they shall be respectfully approached. Those who, despite their wealth, remain miserly and unwilling to give shall be passed by. Donations of any amount, whether large or small, shall be gratefully received.

**Renovations and Repairs**
Maintenance must never be neglected. Several individuals shall be appointed to conduct regular inspections. Halls, walls, ditches, and pathways—anything damaged shall be repaired immediately. Bricks, tiles, bamboo, timber, nails, iron, and lime shall all be requisitioned from the common treasury. Funds and grain solicited for this purpose shall be prepared in advance, stored in the upper treasury, and reserved for renovations.

**Sweeping and Cleaning**
Before sweeping any hall, water must first be sprinkled to settle the dust. After a brief interval, a broom shall be used to gather dust into piles at intervals of five *chi*, ensuring it is not scattered haphazardly. Otherwise, dust may rise and obscure sacred images, banners, and floral decorations—this must be strictly avoided.

**Grain Management**
For abbeys or temples with grain stores, the accounting shall be handled by the grain office. Whether through exchange, transport, or retention, all decisions must be made in advance to avoid missing delivery deadlines. Trustworthy individuals shall be appointed to oversee transport to the granary, ensuring timely completion to avoid urgent demands.

**Managing Distant Farmlands**
For farmlands located far from the abbey or temple, granaries and storage sheds shall be established to store grain, agricultural tools, and other necessities. Only honest and reliable locals shall be employed as caretakers. Outsiders without proper ties must not be allowed to reside there, lest theft or pilfering occur over time, leading to unforeseen troubles. Careful consideration must be given beforehand to avoid future regrets.

**Collecting Rent**
For abbeys or temples with extensive farmlands, landless peasants shall be recruited as tenants. Agreements must be made in advance, specifying the amount of pure grain rent to be paid. At harvest time, overseers shall be dispatched to the fields to supervise the reaping, threshing, and storage, ensuring the rent is collected in full and on time. Tenants shall be prohibited from privately harvesting the crops, thus preventing any shortfall in rent.

**Lending Grain**
When the common treasury has surplus grain, it may be lent to upright and respectable villagers for their sustenance. At harvest time, the principal along with interest shall be collected as per the agreement. This is not for the treasury’s profit but to assist the local populace in times of need.

**Granaries and Storehouses**
Granaries and storehouses, where the common treasury’s supplies are stored, must be separate from living quarters and not directly connected to avoid the risk of fire. Those in charge must be strict and vigilant.

**Gardens and Vegetable Plots**
Daily vegetables must never be lacking; they should be cultivated diligently. All varieties may be eaten, except for the five pungent vegetables—onion, garlic, leek, *xie*, and *wei*—which Daoist practitioners must not consume. As the *Erya* states: “Those who observe fasts and abstain from the five pungent vegetables refer to these five,” not to fish or meat. Daoists observing fasts must take note.

**Requesting Leave**
When visiting family, calling on friends, or traveling afar, one must request leave. The destination must be verified to ensure no impediments exist before permission is granted. No false pretenses shall be tolerated, nor shall others be brought along, lest disputes arise and the reputation of the Daoist community be tarnished. All must exercise caution.

**Cloud Wandering**
Daoists who travel to seek the truth and inquire into the Dao are called *cloud wanderers*. Daoists are servants of Heaven, and those who align with Heaven naturally ascend—hence the term *cloud wandering*. Such wanderers must be skilled in praying for clear skies or rain, dispelling evil and banishing demons, alleviating disasters and rescuing those in distress. They should also be versed in geomancy, physiognomy, astrology, divination, poetry, calligraphy, and painting before setting forth.

Otherwise, they are but common and vulgar individuals, unworthy of consideration.

**Recommendations**
All heads and officers of abodes and temples must select those of virtue and Daoist attainment to oversee their affairs. The unworthy shall not be protected by personal favor, nor shall the virtuous be envied out of private resentment. All must act with impartiality to command the respect of the community.

**Appointment to Office**
Regardless of whether they come from afar or nearby, those of Daoist attainment and capable of managing affairs shall be invited with propriety into abodes and temples to assume office.

**The Abbot**
Should the chief of an abode or temple commit transgressions, the community shall admonish them thrice. If they fail to reform, they shall step down to make way for one of virtue to succeed them.

**Disciplinary Measures for Daoists in Violation**
All violations shall be addressed by the community. For minor offenses, the wrongdoer shall be admonished face-to-face and urged to reform and cultivate goodness. For offenses beyond the bounds of the law, the *Pure Regulations* shall be applied, and their transgressions recorded in the register. After three recorded offenses, the offender shall be seated at the lowest rank during assemblies and barred from ascending the altar during grand jiao rites. For those whose evil is grave and intolerable, making cohabitation in the abode or temple impossible, the community shall expel them. After their expulsion, a broom shall sweep their traces from the ground, severing all ties to prevent their return.

**Established Disciplinary Methods**
Since ancient times, abodes and temples have maintained disciplinary methods through established regulations. The system of discipline is as follows: for offenses comparable to those punishable by flogging or caning under common law, the penalty shall be halved. For instance, if a commoner’s offense warrants forty strokes, the Daoist *Pure Regulations* impose only twenty, allowing the offender to correct their ways and reflect. For other offenses, the severity shall be measured against the law, and penalties determined accordingly—this is the method of discipline and admonition in the Daoist tradition. However, if an offense is brought before the authorities, the penalty for a Daoist shall be one degree harsher than that for a commoner. Why? Because those who cultivate the Dao are duty-bound to accumulate merit, humble their bodies, and endure shame. To commit an offense is to reveal a lack of true cultivation. Dwelling in grand halls, eating without labor, and enjoying the blessings of heaven and humanity—how can their punishment not be one degree harsher than that of commoners? How can they not be held in awe?

**Design of the Pure Regulations Discipline Ruler**
Constructed of wood, it shall be one *cun* and four *fen* in width, four *fen* in thickness, and three *chi* in length.
**Front:**
**Back:**

In violation of the law—*Edict on Disputes and Admonitions* issued in the second year of Qianfeng under Emperor Gaozong of Tang:

We, having received the Mandate of Heaven and ruling over all under heaven, dare not but revere and exalt the Dao of Heaven to manifest Heaven’s virtue. Recently, Yang Taixuan, the Libationer of Haotian Abbey, and the monk Zongjing of Huayan Monastery have disputed over precedence, and though We have already admonished them, they still refuse to comply. We have examined the ancient canons and commanded scholars to assess their relative merits. Upon careful consideration of the precedence between Dao and Buddhism, and the weight accorded to Chinese and foreign teachings, We have weighed present circumstances against ancient principles, tracing reason to its source.

From the perspective of Heaven and humanity: the Dao is the mother of Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things, while the Buddha attained enlightenment through cultivation of the Dao. The Dao is named after Heaven, whereas the Buddha is ranked among humans—who would dare place a human above Heaven and Earth?

From the perspective of Chinese and foreign: the Daoist teaching was inaugurated by the Yellow Emperor and Laozi of Our Middle Kingdom, while Buddhism originated with the foreigner of Kapilavastu in the Western Regions. The Yellow Emperor and Laozi are the great sages of China, whereas the Buddha is but a virtuous man of the Western barbarians. Judged by the distinction between Chinese and foreign, how can an external teaching be placed above that of Our Middle Kingdom?

From the perspective of temporal precedence: the Dao existed before Heaven and Earth, while Buddhism arose later and flourished afterward. The Dao began before Pangu, whereas the Buddha was born in the reign of King Zhuang of Zhou. Between them lie over three million seven thousand years—who would dare place the era of King Zhuang before that of Pangu?

From the perspective of dynasties: the Dao was expounded in the age of the Yellow Emperor, while Buddhism emerged in the time of Emperor Ming of Han. The Dao preceded the Dragon and Horse, whereas Buddhism arrived at the end of the Metal Rabbit. How can one dare to exalt the Later Han and belittle the Yellow Emperor?

Moreover, according to the old customs of the monastic community, precedence is determined by seniority: even a young monk who enters the temple first must be ranked above an elder who enters later. This is an unchanging principle of monastic discipline, a natural order of Heaven and Earth. Since the Dao precedes Buddhism, what doubt can there be?

Henceforth, after this proclamation, both teachings shall abide by Our decree. Should any dare to disobey and persist in disputation, the gravest offenders shall be punished with the utmost severity, while lesser ones shall be sentenced to hard labor as *chengdan* and *zhong*. Let all officials enforce Our judgment.

*Edict on Prohibiting Cliques and Evil* issued in the first year of Hongdao under Our humble virtue, We have inherited the grand enterprise and recognized that the failure to clarify teachings leads to abuses that endure for ten thousand generations. To know of abuses and not reform them is to share equal culpability. We have broadly sought the details of public opinion and established measures to uphold the great principles.

In the age of the Yellow Emperor, the transmission of the Daoist lineage was already established. By the end of the Han, the teachings of Buddhism first appeared. Their original purpose was to transform the violent into the benevolent and guide the wicked toward goodness. Yet how did matters come to this?

In the first year of Zhongping under Emperor Ling of Han, Zhang Jiao of Julu initially used talismanic water to treat illnesses, relieving suffering wherever he went—his methods were indeed effective and commendable. Within a year, people from all directions flocked to him. Zhang Jiao then became arrogant, coveting the imperial throne. He incited rebellion, styling himself the Grand Worthy Teacher and Heavenly General, while his brothers Zhang Bao and Zhang Liang were named Earthly General and Human General, respectively. They amassed over a thousand followers, all wearing yellow turbans as their insignia, and threw the realm into chaos. The very foundation of the Han nearly collapsed. After their defeat and capture, Zhang Jiao and his clique were executed.

Among the abuses of the Buddhist clergy: during the Tianjian era of the Liang, the monk Faqing and his disciple Li Guibo rebelled, with Faqing declaring himself the Emperor of the Great Vehicle and Li Guibo the Ten Abodes Bodhisattva, the Pacifier of Demons, and King of Han. Their warfare raged across thousands of *li*, with weapons blanketing the fields and conflict persisting for years. The people suffered as if scorched by fire, and the state teetered on the brink of collapse, nearly bringing ruin to the nation.

During the reign of Emperor Taiwu of Wei, the Buddhist devotee Gai Wu and the monks of Chang’an Monastery usurped the title of emperor and rebelled. Upon discovery, they were executed.

In the Daye era of the Sui, dozens of bandits styled themselves as Maitreya Buddha, donning *kunlu* hats, burning incense, and carrying flowers. They stormed the Jianguomen Gate, seized weapons from the palace guards, and suddenly erupted into rebellion. As soldiers clashed in the palace courtyards, blood flowed upon the steps, nearly precipitating a great disaster—how terrifying!

In the first year of Wude under Our High Ancestor, the monk Gao Tansheng and five thousand fellow monks rebelled, declaring himself the Emperor of the Great Vehicle. The nun Zheng Xuan was named his Empress Yeshu, and he changed the era name to *Falun*. They attacked prefectures and counties, slaughtering officials and soldiers.

More recently, the monk Zhi Jue married the daughter of the Tao family to fulfill the "peach and plum repentance," colluding with the Khitans to proclaim himself emperor. Another Buddhist devotee, Tie Yu, incited rebellion, declaring the advent of the Holy Buddha and styling himself the Radiant Great Sage Emperor. They killed government troops and burned civilian homes.

From these examples, it is clear that the two teachings have brought no small calamity to the realm. We have examined the ancient records from the dawn of time to the present—spanning hundreds of thousands of years—and found that the Dao has committed but one offense, and its harm was minor. From the time of Emperor Ming of Han to the present, less than a thousand years have passed, yet the harms of Buddhism have been numerous and their rebellions severe.

We aspire to revive the customs of high antiquity and restore the governance of Fuxi and Shennong. Let the people of China follow the teachings of China, and the barbarians follow the teachings of the barbarians. We wish to sever the traces of foreign influence in China to prevent future disasters, to distinguish between the orthodox and the heterodox in the Dao, and to maintain the distinction between Chinese and foreign. How can one, with the heart of a sage, practice the teachings of foreign barbarians? How can We indulge them with leniency and refrain from abolishing them?

Henceforth, the quotas for Daoist and Buddhist clergy shall be fixed. Should any dare to privately recruit disciples, harbor fugitives, deceive the good, or form cliques to imitate one another, they shall be seized by the people and delivered to the judicial authorities for investigation. If any act against the law, their entire clan shall be executed without mercy. Thus, this edict.

**Court Audience Ritual**

For all court assemblies, the Celestial Master shall don the ritual vestments, wear the *fuyang* cap, and hold the inscribed tablet with tassels, as though standing before the Supreme Emperor. All Daoists, regardless of age or rank, shall wear robes of golden hem and the *Zhengyi* cap, holding inscribed tablets. On ordinary days when appearing before the emperor, they shall not hold tablets but wear plain robes, without distinction between Quanzhen and other traditions, their hemp sleeves as usual.

Consider the example of Buddhist monks: regardless of age, rank, or status, all don golden-hemmed robes and cassocks. How then can Daoists, when entering court to see the sovereign, fail to observe proper ritual?

Since the Han dynasty, the greatest taboo has been the *leijin* headwrap and short jacket. The short jacket is the garb of the vulgar masses; the *leijin*, the headwrap of rustics. Recluses in the mountains use it to cover their unkempt hair. Those who cultivate the Great Way preserve spirit and gather *qi*, uniting the Three Flowers at the *niwan* to guard against its opening, lest wind and evil assail it. How can one wear a *leijin* to court? Such conduct is deemed irreverent.

To leave the household is named for the hairpin and cap. To forgo the hairpin and cap in favor of a *leijin*—is this proper? Only the shallow and ostentatious would do so; those of pure virtue would never abandon the ritual of hairpin and cap. Formerly, Duke Zhuang of Zheng killed his son Zang because his cap was askew. Thus did Confucius begin the *Spring and Autumn Annals* with this account. All who belong to the Daoist tradition must take heed.

See also the chapter on "Regulations for Robes and Ritual Attire."

According to a directive issued by the Central Secretariat in the fourth year of Zhongtong (1263) to all circuit branch secretariats: Since the collapse of the Song and the chaos that engulfed the realm, ritual and law have yet to be fully restored, and those who remain obstinate in their ways are none other than Buddhist monks and Daoists. Upon examination, monks take tonsure as their practice, while Daoists leave the household by wearing the hairpin and cap. Yet today, when monks meet their superiors or teachers upon entering a temple, they remove their hats, but when entering the palace gate, they do not remove them—only before the sovereign do they doff their hats. Is this proper ritual?

Moreover, since ancient times, Buddhist and Daoist officials have had no precedent for wearing caps and sashes; their office is marked solely by tonsure or the hairpin and cap. Even the Three Dukes and Six Ministers remove their caps and kowtow before the sovereign. Those who have left the household to cultivate the Way take humility as their foremost virtue and modesty as their power. How can they refuse to remove their hats, acting with arrogance and self-importance?

Henceforth, any monk or Daoist who fails to remove their hat when meeting an official shall receive seventeen strokes of the cane and be returned to lay life. Those who fail to remove their hats when entering court shall receive seventy strokes and be exiled to Tubo. As for attire:

Daoists shall not wear Mongol-style sleeveless jackets but shall don the caps and robes of the Yellow Emperor’s lineage. Monks shall not adopt Tang-style dress, nor wear the lascivious robes of Empress Wu or the *kunlu* hat. Ordained monks shall wear the red woolen robes of the Western Regions; Chan monks shall wear the black cloth robes of Emperor Wu of Liang, and shall not don garments of silk, damask, gauze, or brocade.

Nuns shall be permitted only the attire of female Daoists. Neither lay nor ordained women shall be allowed to live in communal cloisters. Those who conceal male monks, blurring distinctions between male and female, and thus spread licentiousness—leading to the discovery of infant corpses and skeletal remains piled in rear gardens—have been numerous. Such depravity corrupts public morals and must be strictly prohibited. Those who dare persist in such violations shall be executed.

1. **Morning and Evening Hall Services**
   The bell and drum shall be sounded for morning and evening assemblies. The morning service shall begin at first light, the evening service after sunset. Daoists shall first strike the assembly drum to gather the community, then burn incense and recite scriptures once through. After two bows, they shall withdraw. Those who fail to attend shall be fined five *jin* of oil, equivalent to fifty prostrations. Those who spread dissent or refuse to comply shall be fined ten *jin* of oil, equivalent to one hundred prostrations. Those who persist in defiance shall be flogged forty times before the assembly, barred from communal meals and lodging, and their rations withheld for one year.

2. **Discipline for Daoists**
   For minor transgressions, Daoists shall be penalized with offerings of incense or lamps. For graver offenses, they shall be flogged. If they persist after flogging, they shall be assigned to kitchen duties or labor in the gardens. If they transgress again, they shall be reduced to latrine attendants, tasked with cutting rods for latrine use, for a term of one or two years, until they reform.

3. **Expulsion for Grave Offenses**
   For Daoists guilty of repeated or severe misconduct—such as refusing instruction, violating monastic rules, theft, harm, or violent and unbenevolent acts—the gravest penalty shall be permanent expulsion from their home abbey. Their offenses shall be recorded in the abbey’s registry for posterity.

4. **Welcoming Wandering Daoists**
   When wandering Daoists, drawn by the excellence of mountains and rivers or the numinosity of blessed lands, seek to cultivate the Dao at an abbey, their needs shall be provided for equally with resident Daoists. No distinctions shall be made.
