Those who spread slander, defame others, or stir up discord shall receive twenty strokes of the cane and be demoted to work as estate laborers, barred from entering the abbey.

5. **Hospitality to Itinerant Daoists**
   When wandering Daoists arrive at an abbey, they shall be hosted for ten days, with all necessities provided without fail. If they wish to stay through winter or summer, they may do so. If they choose to remain indefinitely, they may also do so. Those of true ability...

If they possess true abilities, they may be permitted to take on duties. When assigning roles, those lacking in pure virtue or prone to rash temper shall not be allowed, lest they provoke misfortune.

**Receiving Itinerant Monks**
When wandering Buddhist monks arrive at an abbey and wish to stay temporarily, observe the summer retreat, or pass the winter, their food and lodging shall be provided without fail from the abbey’s stores. However, their ordination certificates must first be examined to verify their authenticity before they are permitted to stay.

**Register of Wandering Daoists**
Every abbey shall keep a register titled *Record of Wandering Daoists*. When Daoists from afar arrive, the abbey’s superintendent shall inspect their ordination certificates and record in the register: “Daoist [Name], from [Prefecture] [Location], arrived on [Year] [Month] [Day].” When they depart, the date of their departure shall also be recorded, lest fugitives conceal their identities and burden the abbey. If they lack ordination certificates but possess official documents, these shall likewise be examined. They may not be allowed to stay long, but their details must still be recorded in the register, lest the abbey be implicated in harboring fugitives. This record shall serve as reference for verification.

*This concludes the first fascicle of* The Jade Register of the Supreme Dao of the Celestial August, *section on “Abbey Halls and Sacred Altars.”*

The term *gong* (宫) refers to the palatial residences where emperors and kings reside, known as *gong*. The Yellow Emperor offered sacrifices to the Supreme Deity at the *Haotian Gong* (Palace of the Vast Heaven) and to the Three Pure Ones at the *Taiji Tiangong* (Palace of the Great Ultimate Heaven). Thus, when the Yellow Emperor received the Mandate of Heaven, all subsequent rulers who governed the realm were said to "serve Heaven and uphold the celestial mandate"—this marks the origin of *gong*.

The term *guan* (观) combines *xia yang shang* (下仰上, "gazing upward from below") and *yin guan xia* (音贯, "overseeing from above"). It denotes the halls where emperors administered governance, such as the *Mingtang* (Hall of Illumination), embodying the principle of "residing on high to oversee below"—hence the term *guan*. According to the *Inner Biography of the Yellow Emperor*, the Queen Mother of the West bestowed upon the Emperor the Primordial True Form of the White Jade Yuan Shi, which was placed atop a lofty *guan*—this marks the origin of *guan*.

The term *dian* (殿) is explained in the *Yanyi*: "Dian means *fa* (法, 'model' or 'standard')." It signifies the central structure among clustered halls, resolving doubts as noted by Zhi Yu. The *Yaozhu* states: "A *dian* has steps and a raised platform, while a *tang* (堂) has steps but no platform." The *Spring and Autumn Annals* refers to it as *luzin* (路寝), and both the *Book of Rites* and *Baihu Tong* call it "the hall of the Son of Heaven." The *Records of the Grand Historian* notes that Qin Shi Huang constructed the *Chaogong* (朝宫) south of the Wei River, beginning with the *Qiandian* (前殿) and *Efang* (阿房). The *Book of Lord Shang* also mentions "the *dian* of the Son of Heaven," indicating that this designation existed as early as the reign of Duke Xiao of Qin. The Qin dynasty, like the sun, had its *dian*. Later generations, when worshipping the Celestial Emperor, established halls such as the *Jinque Liaoyang Baodian* (Golden Watchtower of the Luminous Yang Treasure Hall), *Wuji Baodian* (Infinite Treasure Hall), and *Taiji Dian* (Hall of the Great Ultimate), with countless names—this marks the origin of *dian*.

The term *ge* (阁) is mentioned in the *Han Shi Waizhuan*: "During the time of the Yellow Emperor, the phoenix nested in the *A Ge* (阿阁)." Thus, *ge* also originated with the Yellow Emperor. Later generations built halls to worship the Celestial Emperor, constructing *ge* such as the *Tongming Ge* (Hall of Universal Illumination), *Zixu Ge* (Purple Void Hall), and *Yuhuang Ge* (Jade Emperor Hall)—this marks the origin of *ge*. I myself have built the *Shouxing Ge* (Hall of the Star of Longevity) and *Tianhuang Ge* (Celestial August Hall) at the *Changsheng Gong* (Palace of Longevity) to pray for the people’s well-being and bountiful harvests.

The term *lou* (楼) is recorded in the *Records of the Grand Historian*, where fangshi (方士) told Emperor Wu of Han: "The Yellow Emperor constructed the Five Cities and Twelve Towers to await divine beings." The Emperor then ascended the *Ganlou* (干楼). Thus, *lou* likely originated in the time of the Yellow Emperor. Later generations, when worshipping the Celestial Emperor, built many *lou* in abbeys and halls, such as the *Zhonggu Lou* (Bell and Drum Tower) and *Qizhen Lou* (Tower of Perfected Immortals)—this marks the origin of *lou*.

The *Daozang* (道藏, Daoist Canon) began when Laozi served as the *Shoucang Shi* (Keeper of the Archives) for King Wen of Zhou, storing books in the Eastern *Guan*. Later Daoist traditions all maintained *Daozang* collections of scriptures. Master Lupi constructed a rotating wheel suspended in a *ge*, which would sound bells and drums when moved. Inside, it housed scriptures, while the exterior was carved with images of celestial deities—this marks its origin. Later generations established repositories such as the *Yunzhang Baoshi* (Cloud Seal Treasure Chamber) and *Tianzhang Baocang* (Celestial Seal Treasure Repository), as well as the *Feitian Falun* (Flying Celestial Dharma Wheel)—this marks the origin of *zang* (藏). I have renamed the *Zang Dian* (Repository Hall) as the *Zongji Dian* (Hall of the Turning Mechanism), signifying its operation of the celestial Dao, like the *Xuanji* (璇玑) and *Yuheng* (玉衡), to align with the heavens.

The term *tang* (堂) refers to the *Yanfa Tang* (Hall of Dharma Exposition) built by Daoist priests (*yuliu*) for celestial *jiao* (醮, ritual offerings). The hall was arranged with six curtains, within which the six masters were seated to preside over the ritual—this marks the origin of *tang*. I have renamed it the *Taixuan Dian* (Hall of the Great Mystery).

The term *Tianguan* (天关, Celestial Gate) originates with me. I established the *Yuchen Tianguan* (Jade August Celestial Gate) at the *Nanji Changsheng Gong* (Southern Culmen Palace of Longevity), with the Four Celestial August Lords arrayed on either side: the first, *Chengtian Fuxiao Qingling Dijun* (Celestial August Lord of the Azure Numen, Assistant to Heaven and Teacher of the Dao); the second, *Anbang Zhuguo Haoling Dijun* (Celestial August Lord of the Radiant Numen, Pillar of the State and Protector of the Realm); the third, *Baozhen Guozuo Danling Dijun* (Celestial August Lord of the Cinnabar Numen, Guardian of the State and Stabilizer of the Mandate); and the fourth, *Zhiyu Wanshen Xuanling Dijun* (Celestial August Lord of the Mysterious Numen, Controller of the Myriad Spirits). Before the gate, two couplets are inscribed:
*"The celestial gates swing open, as immortals from the Three Realms come to pay homage to Heaven;*
*The vault of heaven clears, as the Four Numinous Lords stand in attendance."*

The term *Tianmen* (天门, Gate of Heaven) is recorded in the *Huangtu Yaoji*: "The Yellow Emperor created doors and windows." Thus, the construction of doors and windows followed the establishment of roofs and eaves, serving to open and close at dawn and dusk to ward off violence—hence the term *men* (门). Ancient designs featured pairs of Dragon and Tiger Lords or Celestial Ding (天丁). In my own designs, any gate with Celestial Ding or Dragon and Tiger Lords is called *Tianguan*, while those without are called *Tianmen*, as they serve as the place to worship the Supreme Deity and the Two Pure Ones, situated within the realm of the *Tailuo Yuqing* (太罗玉清, Great Net of Jade Purity). Thus, they are named *Tianmen*. The unenlightened common folk mistakenly call them *Shanmen* (山门, Mountain Gate) or *Sanmen* (三门, Triple Gate), which strays far from the Dao.

The *Boting* (钵堂, Alms Bowl Hall) is where Daoist companions (*luánchóu hèlǚ*) from the four directions gather to lodge. Since ancient times, it has existed in sacred mountains and immortal sites. I constructed a *Boting* at the *Nanji Changsheng Gong*, naming it the *Bingzhen Guan* (Hall of Perfected Immortals’ Lodging). Its name is inscribed on the lintel, with a couplet:
*"All cloud-roaming and water-wandering immortals dwell here;*
*The foremost of the Perfected in the world reside within this first gate."*

Above the front veranda hangs a plaque reading *"Hualing Yinai"* (华灵隐霭, "Misty Veil of the Flourishing Numen"). The veranda’s pillars bear couplets:
*"Expounding the great Dao of the sages of China;*
*Inheriting the legacy of the immortals of the heavens above."*

In the center of the veranda stands a plaque for opening and closing the gate. Within, an image of Perfected Wang Chongyang is enshrined, with a table displaying the Five Phases (Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, Earth) to harmonize their transformations. Seven golden lotuses are arranged in front to symbolize the profound mystery of the Seven Perfected. A *Bojia* (钵架, alms bowl stand) is placed in the center, with a water cauldron below and five bells and one alms bowl suspended above. The stand bears a couplet:
*"When the five bells chime in unison, the gate of the Great Ultimate swings wide;*
*When the single bowl briefly rests, one re-enters the chamber of the Great Mystery."*

On either side, fourteen seats are arranged in pairs, each designated by the cyclical signs *zi, chou, yin, mao, chen, si, wu, wei, shen, you, xu,* and *hai*. The hall’s pillars display couplets:
*"Silently paying homage to the Supreme Deity, ascending to the Golden Watchtower;*
*Quietly guarding the Central Yellow, sealing the Jade Gate."*

There are two side chambers: the left is called *Hechao* (鹤巢, Crane’s Nest), and the right is called *Linsou* (麟薮, Unicorn’s Marsh).

Within are fourteen cloud beds, fully furnished with all necessities for daily practice—robes, ritual implements, and utensils for vegetarian meals—prepared to accommodate itinerant Daoist adepts. Those who establish alms halls should take this as their model.

**Enclosed Chamber (圓室)**
Constructed of brick, the chamber measures one *zhang* in diameter, with no door, only a small aperture for passing food. At the rear, a hole is left for waste disposal. When a Daoist enters, the opening is sealed with bricks—this is called *closing the pass*. After a hundred days of seclusion, the seal is removed—this is called *opening the pass*. Such is the design of the enclosed chamber.

Zhang Daoling, a descendant of Han Dynasty Marquis of Liu (Zhang Liang) from Pei’s Fengyi district, was originally a scholar of the Imperial Academy. Having passed the examinations for *Xianliang Fangzheng* (Worthy and Upright) and *Zhiyan Jijian* (Candid Remonstrance), he later lamented, *"This does nothing to prolong life."* He then pursued the Dao of longevity, mastering the *Nine Cauldrons Elixir Method* of the Yellow Emperor. Entering Mount Heming in Shu, he sat in the enclosed chamber, refining his will and composing twenty-four scrolls of Daoist scripture. After three years, his elixir complete, he told his disciple Wang Chang: *"The divine elixir, when consumed, must ascend to heaven. Yet I must first benefit the nation and relieve the people’s suffering, serving the Three Realms—only then will I be without shame."* The Emperor, hearing of his virtue, appointed him Grand Tutor and enfeoffed him as Marquis of Jixian. On the ninth day of the ninth month in the first year of *Yongshou* (155 CE), he ascended to heaven with his wife, Lady Yong. The enclosed chamber originated with him.

**Hermitage (庵)**
Kuang Xu, a descendant of the Xia Emperor Yu, had five brothers, all born with divine intelligence. The eldest was named Xu Zhou. During King Wu’s reign, he and Laozi served together as *Zhuxia* historians, receiving Laozi’s teachings. He later withdrew to Xunyang, where three brothers built huts on Tianchi Peak, and two on Tiger Creek. Upon achieving transcendence, the mountain was named *Kuanglu* (Mount Lu). Emperor Wu of Han enfeoffed him as *Daming Jun* (Lord of Great Brilliance). The present dynasty later changed *Jun* to *Gong* (Duke). Thus began the hermitage.

Later, reclusive scholars and those who attained the Dao built thatched huts among cliffs and ravines, following this tradition. I, too, have emulated this design, constructing a thatched hut on the southern peak of Mount Xialing. The land produces *lingzhi* mushrooms, so I named it *Zhi An* (Mushroom Hermitage). Its couplet reads:
*"I dwell beyond the world, apart from the vulgar;*
*Who believes this hut holds another heaven?"*

Inside the hermitage, another couplet hangs:
*"A room no larger than a bushel, yet the heart spans the heavens;*
*In silent sitting, spirit congeals—profound thoughts touch the mysterious workings."*

Below the hermitage stands the *White Cloud Elixir Chamber*. Its couplet declares:
*"Grasping white clouds to refine the Great Return;*
*Fleeing the red dust to become a minor recluse—*
*awaiting one of the Dao to dwell here."*

The path begins south of the *Feisheng* (Ascension) Terrace, west of Ling Peak, winding through a grove of pines and cedars. Upon arrival, a plaque reads: *"Deep Within the White Clouds."* Its couplet proclaims:
*"A gourd contains a small heaven and earth;"*

Amidst boundless wild clouds, depths unknown.

As one nears the hermitage, a plaque along the path reads: *"Where Red Dust Never Reaches."* Its couplet proclaims:
*"In stillness, sun and moon stretch long;*
*In leisure, heaven and earth expand wide."*

**Terrace**
In ancient times, those who cultivated the Dao and achieved perfection, fully attaining ascension, would conceal themselves in sacred mountains and immortal lands before ascending. Thus, I have established the *Feisheng* (Ascension) Terrace atop the *Xialing* Grotto-Heaven’s *Xialing* Peak, awaiting those who attain the Dao and join the ranks of immortals—surely they shall mount this terrace to ascend. Upon the terrace’s ancient pillars hangs this couplet:
*"Ninefold elixir perfected, one step soars to the Purple Ultimate;*
*Great Return refined, the body leaps straight to the Azure Heavens."*

**Temple**
The term *temple* denotes the rank of the Six Ministers, the station of a court official, where the *dafu* (grand master) resides. Thus, the Qin established the *Taichang* Temple to preside over the rites of heaven’s worship. Emperor Wu of Han appointed the Daoist Ling Taichong as *Taichang* Minister, renaming the *Taichang* Temple as *Chengtian* Temple to venerate the Supreme Emperor. From then on, Daoist priests served as *Taichang* officials to oversee sacrificial rites. Additionally, a *jijiu* (libationer) was appointed to manage *jiao* (offering) affairs, and the *Chengtian* Temple became the official residence of Daoist priests.

**Shrine**
The shrines of Daoist ancient sages began with the shrine of the Heavenly Perfected Sovereign on Mount Emei. Of old, there were the Three Mountain Shrines. On Mount Emei in Shu, the palace of the Heavenly Perfected Sovereign once stood. At Tianchi and Huxi of Mount Lu in Xunyang, the two shrines of the Great Illuminated Lords were located. On the Five Terraces Mountain of Jin, there were two shrines: one was the palace of the Purple Mansion’s Gate Lord, and the other was the palace where Wang Zijin’s younger brother, Meishou, cultivated the Dao. Each of the Three Mountains possessed luminous stones that, when struck by sunlight amidst white clouds, emitted rays of five colors. After the Five Barbarians’ chaos in the Central Plains, these lands were thrown into turmoil. Because of their radiance, the stones were seized by Shi Le and Yao Chang to build barbarian temples, and thus the Three Mountain shrines of China’s divine beings were extinguished.

**Abode**
The Heavenly Perfected had the *Xuanguan* (Mystic Lodge).

The residences of those who cultivate the Dao are called *Xuanguan* (Mystic Lodges).

**Courtyards**
The dwellings where Daoist communities gather are called *yuan* (courtyards).

**Heavenly Palace**
The chamber where the Crown Prince of King Mu of Zhou revered the Heavenly Venerable Supreme Lord was named the Heavenly Palace. From the Han and Jin dynasties onward, all households that revered the Dao established chambers for the Heavenly Venerable Supreme Lord, all called Heavenly Palaces.

**Thunder Altar**
The chambers where those practicing Thunder Rites and offering prayers revered the Thunder Deity are called Thunder Altars.

**Altars and Sacred Grounds**
Fang’s commentary states: "Sacrifices to the earth are performed on *shan* (sacred grounds), while those to heaven are conducted on *tan* (altars)." The *Book of Documents* records that when King Wu fell ill, the Duke of Zhou constructed three altars on a shared sacred ground. The *Inner Transmission of the Yellow Emperor* records the earliest instance of constructing altars and sacred grounds.

**Imperial Altar**
The liturgical texts state: "Solemnity, reverence, and secrecy pertain to the inner truth of harmonizing and merging with the perfected; the waving of banners, standards, and pennants, with their measured movements, pertain to the external rites. Yet when sincerity unites with the real, and application is unquestioned, one becomes a companion to heaven and earth. Even so, external rites are still required to transform the realm. Every bow and rise, every advance and retreat, all that the eyes see and ears hear—none is not a proper matter. With no opening for wayward thoughts to enter, one may communicate with the Heavenly Perfected and reach the Supreme Lord. Thus, modeling heaven, emulating earth, and reflecting the majesty of the world, one ensures that reverence prevails in all directions."

All altars and sacred grounds are constructed in three tiers, reflecting the distinction between the exalted heaven and the humble earth.

The upper tier is established as the Heavenly Treasure Flower Terrace of the Jade Capital Mountain of the Vacuous August, where the Three Treasures and Imperial Masters are enshrined. To the left, the banner of the Heavenly Perfected and Life-Demolishing Deity is erected; to the right, the standard of the Lion and Evil-Repelling Deity is raised. On the far left, talismans for communicating with the perfected are placed to summon a thousand truths; on the far right, talismans for reaching the numinous are set to invoke ten thousand spirits. In the center, talismans of the Three Mornings are arranged to circulate the myriad *qi* and dispel malevolent vapors.

To the east of the altar, the Azure Dragon banner is planted; to the west, the White Tiger banner talisman; to the south, the Vermilion Bird banner talisman; and to the north, the Dark Warrior banner talisman. In the five directions, stabilizing jade talismans are also laid. This constitutes the upper tier.

If three tiers are not used, the arrangement may be set within a hall or palace according to the directions.

The middle tier establishes eight gates:
- Northwest: the Gate of Cloudy Jade Vacuity for Communicating with the Perfected
- Due north: the Gate of Clear and Cold Mysterious Unity
- Northeast: the Gate of Tranquil and Natural Stability
- Due east: the Gate of Azure Florescence and Primordial Birth
- Southeast: the Gate of Pure Harmony and Vigorous Yang
- Due south: the Gate of Pure Yang’s Radiant Brilliance
- Southwest: the Gate of Earth’s Compliance and Metallic Harmony
- Due west: the Gate of Firm Clarity and Luminous Splendor

The lower tier arranges the twelve *qi*:
- *Zi* position: the *qi* of Dark Heaven’s Gathering Beginning
- *Chou* position: the *qi* of Northern Primordial Naturalness
- *Yin* position: the *qi* of Dispelling Wrong and Sweeping Away Evil
- *Mao* position: the *qi* of Primordial Flourishing Azure
- *Chen* position: the *qi* of Yellow Numinosity and Lofty Mystery
- *Si* position: the *qi* of Tranquil Numinosity and Serene Peace
- *Wu* position: the *qi* of Fiery Perfection’s Descending Brilliance
- *Wei* position: the *qi* of Central Unity and Congealed Perfection
- *Shen* position: the *qi* of Profound Harmony and Solemn Clarity
- *You* position: the *qi* of Firm Solidity and Pristine Harmony
- *Xu* position: the *qi* of True Primordial Nurturing Spirit
- *Hai* position: the *qi* of Returning Yin and Reverting to Perfection

With these three tiers, the altar is complete.

The establishment of the Imperial Altar is not solely for delivering sentient beings. It is also used for submitting petitions and requesting mandates, paying homage to the sages and attending the perfected, averting disasters and repenting transgressions, praying for longevity and seeking blessings for the state and the people, recommending and elevating ancestral spirits, refining and delivering the departed, and beseeching heaven through the construction of jiao rites. All such matters should employ it.

*Illustration of the Three-Tiered Imperial Altar*
*Protocol for Revering the Sages*

**Divine Titles of Heavenly Deities**

*The Book* states: Heaven is called *shen* (spirit), Earth is called *qi* (deity), and humans are called *gui* (ghosts).

Heaven is called *shen* because it is the pure yang, azure, numinous *qi*—spontaneously generating and transforming, concentrating the essence of *qi*, hence the name *shen*. Earth is called *qi* because it receives the numinous essence of heaven, earth, seas, and mountains, embodying the wonder of spontaneity, hence the name *qi*. Humans are called *gui* because all mortals, upon death, are termed *gui*. Even those who cultivate the Dao and attain perfection cannot escape death, leaving dry bones among the living. Though exalted, are they not ultimately still called *lower ghosts*? Thus, they are termed *gui*.

The supreme spirits of the heavens are honored as *Heavenly Venerables*.

**Yuanshi Tianzun (Primordial Heavenly Venerable)**
The Lord of Creation, the origin of all beings. His true name is unknowable; Laozi provisionally called him the *Dao*. Thus, his divine title is established as *Yuanshi* (Primordial Beginning).

**Haotian Yuhuang Shangdi (Jade Emperor of the Vast Heaven)**
This is Heaven itself. Lacking a proper name, it is called *Haotian* (Vast Heaven). The Yellow Emperor honored him as *Yuhuang* (Jade Emperor), dwelling in the highest heaven, hence also called *Shangdi* (Supreme Emperor).

**Houtu Huangdiqi (August Deity of Earth)**
The mountains, rivers, seas, and peaks must each have a spirit to govern them. Lacking a proper name, the Yellow Emperor honored her as *Houtu Huangdiqi* (August Deity of Earth).

**Sun**
The Yellow Emperor honored him as *Taiyang Dijun* (Great Yang Emperor Lord).

**Moon**
The Yellow Emperor honored her as *Taiyin Huangjun* (Great Yin August Lord).

**Beichen (Northern Culmen)**
The pivot of heaven, master of all phenomena. All stars revolve around it, occupying the *zi* position, hence the title *Beichen* (Northern Culmen).

**Five Directions and Five Emperors**
Heaven has five stars corresponding to the Five Phases. The spirits of the Five Phases govern the five directions:
- Eastern spirit: *Qingling Dijun* (Azure Numinous Emperor Lord)
- Southern spirit: *Danling Dijun* (Vermilion Numinous Emperor Lord)
- Western spirit: *Haoling Dijun* (Radiant Numinous Emperor Lord)
- Northern spirit: *Xuanling Dijun* (Mysterious Numinous Emperor Lord)
- Central spirit: *Yuanling Dijun* (Primordial Numinous Emperor Lord)

**Nine Chen (Nine Celestial Sovereigns)**
The Nine Chen are:
1. *Changsheng Dadi* (Great Emperor of Longevity)
2. *Qinghua Dadi* (Great Emperor of Azure Splendor)
3. *Puhua Tianzun* (Heavenly Venerable of Universal Transformation)
4. *Leizu Dadi* (Great Emperor Ancestor of Thunder)
5. *Dayi Tiandi* (Heavenly Emperor of Great Unity)
6. *Dongyuan Dadi* (Great Emperor of the Abyssal Cavern)
7. *Liubo Dijun* (Emperor Lord of the Six Waves)
8. *Kehan Zhenjun* (Perfected Lord of Approval)
9. *Caifang Zhenjun* (Perfected Lord of Investigation)

They are the nine *qi* transformations of *Yuanshi*, hence titled the *Nine Chen Shangdi* (Nine Celestial Supreme Emperors), who act on behalf of Heaven to govern creation and rule all spirits.

**Protocol for Revering the Sages**
*Illustrations of Sacred Images*

**Sacred Images in China**

In China, divine images were traditionally carved from jade—such as the white jade likeness of the Primordial True Countenance placed in the lofty observatory by the Yellow Emperor. Later generations, lacking jade, resorted to clay sculpture. As clay corresponds to the central earth, it is adorned with the five colors to encompass the attributes of the five directions, thereby rectifying the *qi* of the Middle Kingdom.

Images of deities of the eastern direction are carved from wood, while those of the western direction are cast in metal. Images of the southern direction also employ wood, for wood is born of fire. Images of the northern direction are likewise cast in metal, as metal is born of water. All take their forms from their respective correspondences.

**Sacred Thrones**
The throne of sacred images follows the style of a dragon couch. The screen behind it features dragon heads with mouths holding five-colored knotted silks. The screens must depict a single dragon in full relief; floral or abstract patterns are forbidden. The proper form must adhere to the Chinese tradition.

In the Hall of the Three Clarities, the Nine Chen and Nine Emperors should be sculpted on either side. In the Hall of the Jade Emperor, the Thirty-Two Heavenly Emperors should be placed on both sides. On the walls of these two halls, the assembly of the Ten Thousand Sages paying homage to the Primordial should be painted.

In the Hall of the Dark Emperor, the various deities of the Thunder Department should be sculpted on either side. On the walls within, the deeds of the Dark Emperor’s cultivation of the Dao should be depicted.

In the Hall of the Perfected Lord Xu, the ranks of immortals should be sculpted on either side. On the walls within, scenes of ascending to heaven with one’s entire household—riding simurghs and cranes, mounting the wind and commanding dragons, or floating among immortal companions—should be painted.

In the halls of temples and observatories dedicated to prayers for longevity, the central position should be occupied by the Great Emperor of Longevity. To his left, the Heavenly Venerable of Longevity and Life-Preservation; to his right, the Heavenly Venerable of Lifespan Extension and Destiny Augmentation. On either side, the Southern and Northern Dippers should be placed—on the left, the Southern Dipper for destiny augmentation, and on the right, the Northern Dipper for life extension. The Southern Dipper’s six deities should be complemented by the Star of Longevity to make seven, corresponding to the Northern Dipper.

In the Pavilion of the Star of Longevity, the Stars of Fortune and Emolument should be placed above, with the image of the Star of Longevity sculpted below.

**Dragon Tablets**
Daoist dragon tablets should not be ornately carved. I have reformed their design, making them solid and carved in the manner of jade, with the dragon fully visible on the exterior, its spine forming the border. Gold dragons and five-colored clouds are used to signify the virtue of encompassing the five directions. This innovation is my own.

**Tortoise and Crane Censers**
On either side of the cinnabar steps, pairs of tortoise and crane censers—cast in bronze or iron—should be placed. For all jiao rituals, incense is burned within them to communicate with the immortals, allowing the fragrance to ascend to the heavens above.

**Banners**
The banners used in Daoist rites symbolize the Three Powers of Heaven, Earth, and Humanity. I have reformed their ancient design: the finial is made of paulownia or catalpa wood, adorned with the sun, moon, stars, clouds, and rosy dawn, with an image of the Heavenly Venerable painted above to represent Heaven. The body of the banner bears the names of the Heavenly Emperors, while the base features dragons and phoenixes. The side streamers, shaped like hands, are embellished with flying dragons and phoenixes among clouds, representing Humanity. The pendants, also made of paulownia or catalpa wood, depict mountains, rivers, the great earth, the Five Peaks, and the Four Waterways, representing Earth. Such a design was never seen in antiquity; it originates with me. The common practice of using lotus leaves for finials and lotus flowers for pendants is exceedingly vulgar.

**Sun and Moon Lamps**
Between the pillars of every hall, fish-shaped lamps should be suspended, enveloped in clouds and mist, rising high between the architrave and crossbeam. The left lamp, colored red, symbolizes the sun; the right, white, symbolizes the moon. This innovation is also my own.

**Offering Flowers**
Flowers for offerings are carved from paulownia wood in the form of *lingzhi* mushrooms, symbolizing the auspicious tokens of immortality. A peony stem with five-colored blossoms, nine feet tall and adorned with gold thread, evokes the legend of Han Xiangzi, whose flowers instantly bloomed in five colors—a sign of immortal auspiciousness.

**Offerings of the Five Phases**
The five offerings—Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, and Earth—symbolize the generative and restraining cycles of Heaven and Earth’s creation, aligning with the virtue of the divine.
- **Metal**: Cast in copper or iron ingots, gilded with gold foil. Pure gold is avoided for fear of theft.
- **Wood**: Represented by incense, carved into miniature mountains for display.
- **Water**: Sacred water, contained in a vessel.
- **Fire**: Represented by lamps.
- **Earth**: Yellow soil shaped into a cube with eight facets, carved with the Eight Trigrams for display.

**Offering Fruits**
Fruits for offerings should be carved from wood: peaches of longevity, intertwined pears, fiery jujubes, plums from the Dark Garden, and melons from Kunlun.

**Incense Offerings**
*Jiangzhen* incense is the sacred incense for worshipping the Heavenly Emperor. Apart from this, *chenxiang* and *suxiang* are secondary. *Xinling* incense can convey one’s sincerity to the Heavenly Emperor. Incense to be avoided includes benzoin, frankincense, and sandalwood—foreign concoctions forbidden by celestial law. Utmost caution is required.

**Ingredients for Xinling Incense**
- *Jiangzhen* (5 *qian*)
- *Yujin* (5 *qian*)
- *Chenxiang* (5 *qian*)
- *Suxiang* (5 *qian*)
- *Leixiang* (8 *qian*)
- *Gaoben* (1.6 *liang*)
- *Gansong* (1.6 *liang*)
- *Baizhi* (1.6 *liang*)
- *Lingling* (1.6 *liang*)
- *Dahuang* (2 *qian*)
- *Xiangfu* (2 *qian*)
- *Xuanshen* (2 *qian*)

To compound the incense, on the day before the *jiazi* day, arrange the ingredients in a quiet chamber designated for incense preparation. Set up the tablets of the Five *Zi* Deities, and offer incense and lamps with utmost purity. The five deities presiding are:
- The Spirit of *Jiazi*
- The Spirit of *Bingzi*
- The Spirit of *Wuzi*
- The Spirit of *Gengzi*
- The Spirit of *Renzi*

**Method for Compounding Xinling Incense**
- On the *jiazi* day: Gather the ingredients.
- On the *bingzi* day: Grind them.
- On the *wuzi* day: Mix them together.
- On the *gengzi* day: Form them into pellets.
- Offer them upon the Heavenly Altar on the *renzi* day.
- On the following *jia* day, store them in gourds and hang them up.

Thereafter, burn one pellet daily to worship Heaven. This incense is not for common use; in times of urgent prayer, burning it can invoke the virtue of the divine. If one encounters peril on the road—such as bandits, storms at sea, or other dangers—and lacks fire, chew the incense and spit it upward to avert calamity.

**Celestial Music and Regalia**
Celestial music refers to the orchestral arrangements in the hall. Its origins trace back to the time when the Yellow Emperor convened the assembly of spirits on the western slopes of Kunlun. He commanded Ling Lun to gather bamboo from the Valley of Slackening and craft panpipes, flutes, and reed pipes to summon the phoenix.

The phoenix cried out. He forged bells and chimes to harmonize the pitch-pipes and determine the intercalary months of the year. Taking the hide of Kui, he made drums to offer to the Heavenly Emperor. Then, combining the zither and sehtar crafted by Fuxi, he composed music, whereupon all the spirits gathered, the beasts danced in unison, and the phoenix came in pairs. By the time of Emperor Yao’s grand sacrifice at the Round Mound to worship the Heavenly Emperor, the ancient rites had faded with time, losing their original essence. Emperor Wu of Han then commanded the musician Hou Tiao to craft the konghou harp to match the celestial sounds, revising the music. Later, Daoist masters were ordered to perform the grand *Celestial Music* in the Palace of Vast Heaven to worship the Three Pure Ones, naming it *Music of the Three Pure Ones*.

**Hymn of the Three Pure Ones’ Celestial Music**
Gazing up at the Dragon Chariot of the Vast Heavens,
The Emperor descends to the Jade Palace.
Rainbow banners and feathered canopies follow the celestial beings;
Your servant leads the feathered immortals below to offer reverence.
Witness my sincerity, as my devotion ascends;
I bow my head in homage, praying for the longevity of the people.
May the imperial legacy endure for ten thousand generations,
And the kingdom’s prosperity and blessings know no end.
With this precious incense, I supplicate the boundless heavens,
Universally offering to the Heavenly Sovereign.
A wisp of auspicious smoke coils upward;
The people lift their voices in hope of eternal grace.

**Instruments of the Music**
- *Drum of Thunderous Assembly*: The grand *fengfeng* drum.
- *Key of Azure Sky’s Resonance*: The great bell.
- *Bell of Ten Thousand Gold’s Echo*: Sixteen pitch-responsive bells on the rack.
- *Chime of Jade and Lapis*: Sixteen pitch-responsive chimes on the rack.
- *Cluster of Luminous Jade from the Jasper Pool*: The *taotao* drum.
- *Chime of Truth-Permeating Clarity*: The hand chime.
- *Golden Bell of Jade Sky*: The golden bell.
- *Jade Chime of the Jade Pure Heaven*: The jade chime.
- *Zither of Grand Harmony by Taihao*: The zither.
- *Pipe of Cloud-Quelling Jade*: The reed pipe.
- *Sehtar of the Xiang Spirit’s Ethereal Clarity*: The sehtar.
- *Flute of Celestial Immortal Sounds*: A harp-like instrument with coiled strings.
- *Ruan of Moonlit Jasper Terrace*: The ruan.
- *Cymbals of the Roaring Dragon and Commanding Crow*: The cymbals.
- *Pipes of Azure Flower Jade Heaven*: The sheng.
- *Flute of Cloud-Soaring Wind*: The flute.
- *Drum of the Wind-Roaring Celestial Alligator*: The hand drum, called *Jade Rite*.
- *Clappers of Jasper Terrace’s Jade*: The jade clappers.
- *Gong of Red Sky’s Resonance*: The gong.
- *Filler of Harmonious Tones*: The *tian* (sound-filling instrument).
- *Curtain of Grand Harmony’s Preservation*: The *lian* (harmony-preserving screen).
- *Prayer of Five Tones’ Concord*: The prayer of harmonized notes.
- *Yue of Eight Tones’ Ninefold Completion*: The *yu* (wooden tiger).

This was the music used by the Yellow Emperor to worship the Three Pure Ones. Today, only bells, drums, sheng, xiao, *yun’ao*, and *xianlai* are employed. It is used when ascending the altar to pay homage to the Perfected and audience the Emperor, while for daily rituals and scripture recitations, ordinary immortal music suffices.

**Emperor’s Bell**
When the Yellow Emperor convened the spirits on Kunlun’s peak, the Heavenly Emperor bestowed upon him the *Emperor’s Bell*. Daoists speak of "grasping the Emperor’s Bell, hurling fire for ten thousand miles, and ringing the bells of the eight directions." The Heavenly Soldiers wield it, also calling it the *Fire Bell*. During Emperor Daizong’s reign, the Hu monk Amoghavajra held the *Ullambana* assembly and replaced the chime with the Daoist *Emperor’s Bell*, later modifying its head for use as a bell.

**Cymbals and Handbells**
When Chiyou drove tigers and leopards to battle the Yellow Emperor, the latter forged cymbals and handbells to defeat them, for the beasts feared their sound. They also startle and dispel demons. During Empress Wu’s reign, the monk Huaiyi claimed that during the Buddha’s lifetime, demons—led by Mara—had hidden, served poisoned meals, dug fire pits, smeared the Buddha with filth and blood, and trampled him with elephants. The Buddha sought to destroy them, but the demons declared, "Our kind is vast; we shall later destroy your Way." Thus, Huaiyi used cymbals and handbells in every ritual to startle and expel demons. Empress Wu remarked, "Though cymbals can frighten demons, how can the souls of our ancestors be summoned to partake in offerings? If even demons are startled, how much more so ghosts?" She ordered Daoist rites for the living and the dead to employ them, but their use should be strictly prohibited.

The Heavenly Perfected Sovereign said: "Once, the Primordial Heavenly Worthy expounded the mysterious and wondrous Dharma Gate of the Numinous Treasure, delivering the method of transcendence. He assembled the immortals at the Terrace of Heavenly Treasure in the Void Star Heaven. The celestial processions thronged, with the sun, moon, and stars, the jade armillary sphere, divine beasts, phoenixes, vermilion chariots, green carriages, banners, canopies, and rainbow flags all in attendance. Those who perform the Dao, establishing altars and steps, follow the celestial rites to deliver the people of the lower world. I once witnessed in the void the feathered banners, crimson tassels, dragon flags, and phoenix chariots of the heavenly beings. Emulating their form, I reformed the ancient methods and created the *Immortal Regalia*. Below are their depictions:

- *Banners of the Purified Yellow Path*: One pair
- *Banners of Proclaiming to the Myriad Spirits*: One pair"

- *Banners for Quelling and Subduing the Hundred Demons*: One pair
- *Threads of the Nine August Ones’ Myriad Ages*: One pair
- *Banners of the Three Clarities’ Divine Rainbow*: One pair
- *Pennants of the Supreme Subtlety’s Commanding Spirits*: One pair
- *Pennons of the Great Clarity’s Utmost Mystery*: One set—one side painted with a dragon, the other with a tiger
- *Scarlet Nodes of the Five Spirits’ Crimson Empyrean*: One pair
- *Ruyi Scepters of the Nine Heavens’ Demon-Subduing*: One pair
- *Fans of the Nine Empyreans’ Cloud-Wings*: One pair
- *Fans of the Fragrant Clouds of Yu Luo*: One pair
- *Canopies of the Divine Recitation’s Flying Rosy Clouds*: One pair
- *Lanterns for Dispelling Darkness*: One pair
- *Lanterns of the Crimson Empyrean’s Divine Flame*: One pair
- *Lamps of the Heavenly Thoroughfare’s Fragrant Empyrean*: One pair
- *Incense Pavilion of the Vermilion Phoenix Presenting Auspiciousness*
- *Chariot of the Divine Dragons Bearing the Sage*

The above *Immortal Regalia* are made with vermilion poles and jeweled canopies, adorned with pearl nets, silk, and colored fabrics, meticulously painted and arranged with solemnity. They are arrayed on the four sides of the outer altar. During each morning’s *Step of the Void* procession, Daoist acolytes each hold one piece of the *Immortal Regalia*, harmonizing their *Huaxia* chants as they follow the ranks, circling the imperial altar. They are not to be used except during altar rites and morning audiences, at which times they are arranged on stands at the altar’s four sides.

In later ages, when the Dao’s fortune waned and its teachings declined, these were made of paper, burned at the end of the *jiao* rites like funerary objects—this practice is greatly to be avoided.

- *Furnace for the Heavenly Perfected’s Demon-Commanding*
- *Demon-Commanding Pennon with Six-Face Talismans*

Two-Face, Three-Face, Four-Face, Five-Face, and Six-Face *Lion-Warding Ritual Batons* and *Tally Batons*. The *Pennon for the Heavenly Perfected’s Demon-Commanding* is set to the left of the *Altar of Heavenly Treasure* to ward off evil, while the *Lion-Warding Ritual Baton* is placed to its right. Both are made with vermilion poles and jeweled canopies, their pennon bodies woven with pearl strands and crafted from paulownia wood. The six-faced pennon bears one talismanic inscription per face, lacquered in vermilion and gilded, with all others fashioned according to the same pattern. Whenever the Dharma Master performs the *Step of the Void* procession, two acolytes hold these implements, attending at his left and right, circling in unison. Thus does the Dao command reverence through solemn ritual, and the heart stand in awe before its sacred forms, never straying into delusion or disorder.

Those who cultivate immortality must empty the heart and still the nature, their virtue perfected and their spirit attuned to the True Sovereign. With a single thought, resonance is achieved, and the heart of Heaven merges as one—what need have they for external rites to commune with the Real? Yet since the ancient forms exist, how can they now be abandoned? Thus are they preserved.

*Illustrations of Lanterns for the Altar of Heaven*
The lanterns used in *jiao* altars once numbered over a hundred varieties. Here are recorded ten commonly used types, as the full array is too numerous to include in full. The illustrations are as follows:

*Illustration of the Jade Emperor’s Lantern*
*Illustration of the Lantern of the Celestial Circuit*

*Illustration of the Life-Spirit Lantern*
*Illustration of the Northern Dipper Lantern*
*Illustration of the Southern Dipper Lantern*
*Illustration of the Three Luminaries and Dipper Lantern*
*Illustration of the Nine Heavens Jade Pivot Lantern*
*Illustration of the Fire Virtue Lantern*
*Illustration of the Nine Palaces Eight Trigrams Earth Lantern*
*Illustration of the Blood Lake Hell Lantern*
*Illustration of the Refinement and Salvation Lantern*
*Great Tenuous Yellow Banner of the Supreme Subtlety*
*Banner of the Peerless Spirit of Destiny*

*Complete Perfection Ritual Regulations*
When sitting the alms-bowl ritual, one bowl may accommodate eight, twelve, or twenty-four persons. For those in grand alms-bowl halls, the number may reach a hundred—this is not subject to the same limits.

Four offices are established:
1. The *Master of the Bowl*, with
2. two *Deputy Masters of the Bowl*, who lead the left and right ranks, seated in the upper seats to preside over the proceedings.
Additionally, two *Attendants of the Left and Right Halls* are appointed, seated near the door facing inward to oversee the sitting practice, regulating the timing of entry, exit, rising, and the placement and removal of the alms-bowl.

No patrols of the hall, no enforced sitting, no disciplinary measures, and no reprimands are permitted. Once within the *Circular Hall*, the Daoist assembly engages in the alms-bowl practice out of reverence for the great matter of life and death, refining body and heart to transcend the suffering of mortality and ascend to the ranks of the sagely. How could this be compelled by force or coercion? All such measures are entirely abolished.

Some employ the six periods for sitting the bowl, but excessive practice without proper application often leads to wind-paralysis ailments. It is far better to limit the practice to the four periods of *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*, drawing upon the upright *qi* of Heaven and Earth—this is most efficacious.

Those who enter the enclosure engage in the foundational work of stillness, refining spirit and form. After a hundred days, the practice may begin. Without the oral instructions of a true master, or if one’s own application is insufficient, the practice may instead induce illness, becoming a harmful art. Utmost caution is advised.

The alms-bowl sitting period commences on the fifteenth day of the second month and concludes on the fifteenth day of the fifth month.

From the fifteenth day of the seventh month to the fifteenth day of the tenth month, and from the fifteenth day of the tenth month to the fifteenth day of the first month of the following year. Within a year, only two bowl-sitting periods are observed, as the summer months are too hot for practice.

Outside the bowl hall, a *yunban* (cloud-shaped gong) and a *muyu* (wooden fish drum) are placed. On either side of the central hall, single mats are arranged for sitting, with two small bells positioned in front: the right is called the "movement bell," and the left the "stillness bell." Each day at the *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you* hours, when the appointed time arrives, the assembly enters the hall and takes their seats, awaiting the designated attendant’s strikes to signal the opening and closing of the session, as well as the order of movement and stillness. The daily ritual for entering the hall and taking one’s seat is described below.

A signboard for opening and closing the hall is placed outside the door, inscribed with red characters on a gold plaque. When the senior practitioner is seated, the characters for "closing the hall" face outward, and no one may enter. Upon concluding the session, the sound of the *yunban* serves as the signal, and the characters for "opening the hall" are turned outward.

**Method for Determining the Bowl-Sitting Period**
On clear, sunny days, the bowl is first placed atop the water vessel, and the sundial is used to mark the time. When the shadow just reaches the initial quarter of the *wu* hour, the bowl is set upon the water vessel. By the time the *wu* hour transitions into the *wei* hour—specifically the initial quarter of *wei*—the bowl must sink, causing the five bells to chime in unison, thus marking the full hour before the session may conclude. If the bowl fails to sink at the appointed time, slightly enlarge the water outlet beneath it. If it sinks prematurely, the outlet is too large and must be adjusted to a smaller size, ensuring precise alignment with the hour.

When the bowl is placed at the initial quarter of the *wu* hour, the Daoist assembly takes their seats. By the final quarter of *wu* approaching the initial quarter of *wei*, the bowl sinks into the water, and the five bells resound—only then may the assembly rise. This method ensures the timing remains exact, and future practitioners should follow this as their standard.

**Design of the Bowl Stand**
In ancient times, a small chamber resembling an incense pavilion was constructed, with a *Taiji* circle suspended by a rope from the pavilion’s beam. A bell hung within the circle, and the bowl was struck beneath it. A water vat held the bowl to mark the time.

I have since reformed this system, replacing it with a stand. The stand’s two ends are adorned with colored tassels, and a jeweled canopy hangs at its center. A chime with five bells is installed within, and the bowl strikes the canopy above. When the hour arrives and the bowl fills with water, it sinks, causing all five bells to ring in harmony—a design unlike any found in the secular world, first devised by me.

**Quanzhen Ritual for Entering the Hall**
When a Quanzhen Daoist on pilgrimage first arrives at a temple or abbey and enters the bowl hall, they are led by the Master of Ceremonies to pay respects before the ancestral master. First, three incense offerings are made. After the Master of Ceremonies recites the *Cantang* text, a nine-prostration bow is performed. The practitioner then retires from the hall to join the assembly for tea.

**Cantang Text**
In search of kindred spirits,
I wander lakes and seas.

With staff and bamboo hat, half a lifetime spent in carefree wandering. Unfettered by wild waters and idle clouds, ten years of roaming now weary me. Girded with a sword that props up the heavens, I command all spirits to tremble in the void; embracing a three-foot withered paulownia zither, I strike celestial notes to summon the crisp qi of the firmament—startling gibbons and cranes in the empty mountains, subduing tigers and coaxing dragons to roar over autumn waters. Thus do I dwell beside the Great Void, befriending wind and moon, in such refined seclusion—how magnificent! Truly immortal!

Moreover, with a staff I pierce through boundless green mountains, with my feet I tread across the ten directions’ clouds and waters. Though I have yet to shatter the void, I must forge mortal bones into something transcendent. If an iron pestle can be ground into a needle, how can the Golden Elixir not be achieved? Should the secret strike true and the Dao align with the subtle, what I study is no heterodox or unorthodox deviation, but the Great Way of China’s sages. This path can shatter the demonic barrier, erasing my name from the ledgers of darkness; it can ascend me to the celestial courts, inscribing my name in the Register of Gold. Awaiting the day I soar on azure skies, riding a white crane above the winds of jade; for now, I feast on yellow essence and purple mushrooms, lingering in the valleys and gorges.

I have savored the cool breeze and bright moon, tasting the wonders of the Dao; I have struck golden bells and wielded jade dust-whisks, plumbing the depths of the mysterious. Across the ends of the earth I sought the lofty, yet like unyielding iron I have not met my lodestone; like needle and mustard seed, we have not yet found affinity. Thus today I come especially in search of truth.

By chance I arrive here, daring to trespass upon the ancestral master’s hall. Ashamed of my rustic manners, I humbly offer incense in sincerity, invoking: the Dao Ancestor, the Yellow Emperor; the Dao Ancestor, the Most High Lord Lao; the northern and southern lineages; and all the perfected immortals and sages of past ages. Reverently, I address the esteemed master presiding over the bowl hall, the immortal companions and perfected peers within these jade halls—whose Dao bones are already formed, whose immortal aura has long been manifest. We, mere blades of grass and motes of dust, base as mist and clouds, by the faintest thread of root-qi have been permitted to enter the supreme and wondrous gate. Granted the great honor of ascending the hall, we offer this initial rite of courtesy. For a time, we set aside our gourds and bamboo hats to follow you, discussing the Dao and expounding the mysterious in your company. From this moment of reverence, we humbly beseech your guidance. Together we keep the first watch on cold nights, together we observe the Gengshen vigil, speaking of past and future lives.

With bowed reverence, the ritual concludes, and tea is received.

**Rite for Receiving the Bowl and Settling into a Cell**

Before the alms bowl, arrange the grand incense table, with one large incense burner and two hand censers. The principal bowl-holder, the secondary bowl-holder, and the left and right duty-hall attendants together—with the entire assembly following their ranks—form two rows to stand in reverent alignment. A Dao child is then instructed to deliver the address.

**Address Upon Entering the Garden:**
We bow in reverence, for golden lotuses offer auspicious omens, and the Seven Perfected transmit the profound words of their legacy. Cloud-script radiates its brilliance, and for ten thousand generations, all look up to the wondrous subtleties of the Recluse. The establishment of this garden serves to return to the root and restore the mandate; the seating in the alms-bowl hall serves to abandon delusion and return to truth. When the four great elements align with the seasons, one suddenly realizes the cosmos resides within; when the five bells resound, one discerns that essence and function share a single source. We humbly pray for the silent blessings of heaven and earth, for the workings of creation to aid our refined aspirations, for the mountain spirits to maintain tranquility, and for the realm to remain serene. May the ten directions be pure and flawless today, revealing the hidden and manifesting the subtle—where not even a speck of red dust can reach, and the spirit is refined to return to emptiness. The Dao precedes the Lord of Images; the body abides above the Pure Capital.

Now, we Dao assembly, by the slightest merit of past good deeds, have been permitted to dwell in these renowned mountains. Ashamed that we possess no rare immortal blossoms or true elixir, we can only harbor the idle fancy of a moonlit jade pool. Yet in our eagerness to advance in cultivation, we must not yield to others; though the Dao does not exalt the individual, we must first master ourselves. The grotto-heaven lies on level ground—we watch as phoenixes and cranes are summoned in the azure world. Even our very bodies are but horses and men; we tether the monkey-mind within the garden chamber.

Today, we shall silently ascend to the Celestial Court and mount the Golden Portal, observing stillness once daily to guard the Central Yellow and seal the Jade Pass.

The address concludes.

The left duty-hall attendant, holding a hand censer, escorts the principal bowl-holder to the incense table. Three offerings of incense are made before returning to position. The right duty-hall attendant, holding a hand censer, escorts the secondary bowl-holder to the incense table. Three offerings of incense are made before returning to position. Then, together with the entire assembly, all perform a nine-bow ritual facing upward.

The left duty-hall attendant leads the left row, beginning with the principal bowl-holder down to the lowest seat, settling each into their place. A single bow is made to each before withdrawing, after which one returns to their own seat. The right duty-hall attendant, also holding a hand censer, leads the right row to settle into their seats, following the same ritual—only a single bow is made, without full prostration.

The right duty-hall attendant strikes the bell three times. Once completed, the duty officer presents the seating chart, and the alms bowl is carried out the door. The cloud board is struck three times, and the placard is turned to display "Closed Gate." Returning to the hall, they approach the incense table to offer three major incense offerings. Next, hand-censer incense is offered: holding the censer, they bow once before the principal bowl-holder, then proceed in order to bow once before each member of the assembly. Returning to the incense table, three incense offerings and three bows are made. The left duty-hall attendant then strikes the stillness bell three times, and the duty officer enters their seat to await the sound of the bell. The right duty-hall attendant strikes the bell five times, and the duty officer rises to open the door, striking the cloud board five times. The placard is turned to display "Gate Opened."

**Daily Ritual for Ascending the Hall and Settling into Seats:**
Whenever the hours of *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, or *you* arrive, the duty officer first strikes the wooden fish six times to assemble the assembly for ascending the hall and settling into seats. Once completed, the right duty-hall attendant strikes the bell three times. The duty officer then presents the alms bowl and exits the door, turning the placard to display "Closed Gate." The cloud board is struck three times. Returning to the hall, they approach the incense table to offer three incense offerings and three bows. Next, hand-censer incense is offered: holding the censer, they bow once before their own bowl-holder, then proceed in order to bow once before each seated member of the assembly—no full prostration is performed. Returning to the incense table, three incense offerings and three bows are made. The duty officer then enters their seat. The left duty-hall attendant strikes the stillness bell three times. After the bell’s resonance fades, the right duty-hall attendant strikes the bell five times, and the duty officer rises to open the door, striking the cloud board five times. The placard is turned to display "Gate Opened," and the assembly exits their seats.

**Regulations on Headgear and Robes:**
The headgear and robes of antiquity are those of the Yellow Emperor’s time.

Since the Later Zhao ruler King Wuling altered them to Hu-style garments, China’s attire gradually transformed, until Emperor Yang of Sui, for convenience in hunting expeditions, adopted Hu dress entirely. Only the robes and headgear of Daoist priests remained unchanged, hence the term "Yellow Crown."

**Tongtian Crown**
A design of my own creation. Within, a hair-binding cap is worn; atop it, the Three Terraces. In front, the Southern Dipper; behind, the Northern Dipper. To the left and right, the sun and moon. Above, a crown of curling clouds, secured with tassels. When wearing the Tongtian Robe, this crown is donned—known as the sovereign’s crown, not to be used by any but the king.

**Tongtian Robe**
A design of my own creation. The robe is of dark hue with a yellow lining, comprising an upper garment and lower skirt. On the chest, the Vermilion Bird; on the back, the Dark Warrior; on the left shoulder, the Azure Dragon; on the right shoulder, the White Tiger—representing the Four Spirits, all embroidered in gold. At the front, two sashes of dragon and tiger. The lower skirt has twelve panels, corresponding to the twelve months. The bordered hem evokes the image of cosmic boundaries. This is the raiment of emperors who attain the Dao and ascend in flight—worn only for offering sacrifices to Heaven and serving the gods, not for ordinary men.

**Jiejin Headwrap**
My original design, made of dark fabric. Two styles exist for winter and summer, unrestricted by material—silk or linen—forming a distinct tradition unlike common attire. Not to be worn by any but the king.

**Xuejin Headwrap**
My original design, woven from dark ramie silk, lined with swan down. Worn during snow and bitter cold to protect the head.

**Leijin Headwrap**
The casual headwear of mountain-dwelling Daoists, akin to the attire of rustics. In ancient times, there was no net headwrap; it was worn to contain unruly hair or shield the head from cold. How could such a thing be proper for court audiences before the emperor or for ceremonial occasions? Mountain recluses and cultivators of the Dao, regardless of rank, all wear it. Nowadays, frivolous scholars seeking self-importance don it as if they were officials on duty. Wu Caolu’s poem on the Leijin reads:

*For mountain rustics indolent, a shield from cold—*
*How could it suit those calling on the court?*
*In this degenerate age, customs are impure;*
*Yet fleeing to the Dao’s gate, they don the Zhengyi crown.*

This is precisely the point.

**Net Headwrap**

The net headwrap did not exist in ancient times; it was first created during the Hongwu era of Our Dynasty. Its circular frame is large and distinct from common designs.

**Cap**
The cap’s construction allows for trailing headscarves as adornment. It may be made from lightweight materials like rattan, bamboo, or fine gauze, just as in secular use.

**Lacquered Cap**
Shaped like a cloud hat, its platform is lacquered black, without a covering.

**Rain Hat**
Woven from bamboo sheath or birch bark to shield against rain, dew, and sunlight. The lightest and simplest kind is made from rattan or bamboo roots, which is also quite elegant, though some may find it overly ornate. A larger version that rests on the shoulders is called the "well-frame hat," particularly favored by Quanzhen Daoists and wandering cloud-and-water mendicants.

**Snow Hat**
Hexagonal at the top, with a narrow crown and wide brim. It is covered with black silk or gauze to block wind, sand, and sunlight, used by those seeking the truth or visiting masters during travels.

**Rustic Hat**
Constructed with a bamboo frame, layered with saltwater-boiled *xiao-xie* leaves to repel rain and snow—suitable for mountain dwellers.

**Ritual Robe**

**Yellow Emperor’s Attire**

When the Yellow Emperor beheld the Celestial Beings, he wore a golden lotus crown, its petals rising and bowing above, and a ritual robe adorned with golden stars, constellations, clouds, and rosy dawn. Holding a jade tablet, he advanced and said: "The Emperor toils in heart for all under heaven, governing the people with life-bestowing virtue—this is true power." Thus, he first embodied their form to create the ritual robe, which became the Daoist vestment for worshipping Heaven.

**Shangqing Ritual Robe**
A cloak of purple and azure, purple on the outside and azure within. Its width spans four feet and nine inches, corresponding to the numbers of the four seasons. Its length measures five feet and five inches, aligning with the qi of Heaven and Earth. The outer and inner layers follow one rule: the outer bears twenty-four strips, the inner fifteen, together corresponding to the thirty-nine stations of the Perfected Celestial Emperors. Only when clad in this imperial vestment may one proceed. Without it, one must not recklessly handle the sacred scriptures. This ritual robe is bestowed upon twelve jade lads; it may not be lent to others, nor defiled by impurity, lest the divine vestment be treated with irreverence.

**Female Perfected’s Attire**
A purple gauze robe, two zhang and four chi in length, with sleeves cut to fit comfortably. The sleeves are divided into sixteen strips, the body into twenty-three, totaling thirty-nine strips. Additionally, an azure gauze skirt is made, requiring fifteen chi of fabric, cut into eight panels each four feet and nine inches long. The remaining fabric forms the waistband, divided into eight panels with thirty-two strips—this is the "flying azure skirt." Without this ritual robe, one may not chant the sacred scriptures of Shangqing. This vestment is bestowed upon twelve jade maidens; it may not be lent to others, nor defiled by impurity, lest the divine vestment be treated with irreverence.

**Cave-Dwelling Attire**
Designed by Kou Qianzhi.

**Robe**
The scriptures speak of the "crane-wing robe, frosted and dense." In modern times, people loosely add a border a chi wide, its design identical to the crane cloak.

**Crane Cloak**
Worn by all Daoists, its color unrestricted. Those of profound virtue use black; ordinary Daoists dare not.

**Fur Garment**
In high antiquity, people wore hides and pelts. Daoists often used deer fur for robes, as deer are creatures of transcendence. Later generations favored fox fur.

**Padded Jacket**
A variant name for the *nuo* robe, existing since ancient times. For those who have left the household life or dwell in mountains to cultivate the Dao—unable to escape the cycles of yin and yang or the cold and heat—such garments are essential to ward off wind and chill, and must never be lacking.

**Half-Sleeves**
A short-sleeved garment. Daoists use it as attire for watering flowers and tending herbs.

**Belly Wrap**
Its design resembles the common version, though padded or quilted to suitable thickness. Particularly vital for elderly mountain-dwelling Daoist learners.

**Undershirt**
The garment worn closest to the body must be washed and kept immaculately clean to avoid defilement.

**Short Robe**
A garment reaching only to the waist, called a short robe. For mountain dwellers and those in the wild, it facilitates climbing and traversing. Ancient cultivators of the Dao sometimes likened it to the attire for gathering magic mushrooms.

**Wide Sleeves**
The Daoist scriptures speak of "azure skirts and feathered sleeves, dragon-patterned phoenix robes." In antiquity, there were "radiant flying-cloud sleeves." Today, these are called *chuòxiù*—wide sleeves with a short body, paired with a lower skirt. Those who study the Dao must wear them.

**Trousers**

**Trousers**
Shaped like a skirt, with waistbands and ties at both ends. They must be washed and changed daily. Daoist practitioners who have left the household life should not remove their inner trousers even when briefly resting at night. Whether thickly padded or lightly lined, they must be kept clean and frequently refreshed, regularly infused with fragrant smoke to prevent the accumulation of foul qi.

**Leggings**
Made in single, lined, or padded layers according to the season, they should also be washed and changed frequently.

**Arm Sleeves**
These garments serve two purposes: to maintain cleanliness and to ward off the cold. They are indispensable for Daoist cultivators.

**Leg Wraps**
Now called *xíngchán* (walking wraps). Particularly essential for those who traverse mountains with swift steps, they must be tightly secured.

**Boots**
Boots have rounded toes and wide soles. In ancient times, they were called *xuē*. While suitable for treading through filthy places, they should never be worn indoors. Though commonly used among laypeople, they are inappropriate for mountain dwellers.

**Shoes**
Shoes may be made from wood, bark, bamboo, ramie, hemp, kudzu, or angelica, each chosen as available. However, they should not be crafted from silk, brocade, embroidered fabrics, or patterned leather—excessive luxury is unsuitable.

**Sitting Mats**
Daoist priests and female adepts carry scriptures, precepts, talismans, and celestial writings upon their persons. With true spirits attending their forms and Dao-qi guarding and protecting them, the garments they wear are called *fǎfú* (Dharma robes), revered and safeguarded by divine beings. When resting, they must maintain purity. If venturing among laypeople, they must not allow their sacred presence to be conflated with worldly affairs.

Ordinary people’s mats accumulate considerable foul qi, so each person should have a sitting mat, four *chǐ* square, plainly wrapped with green edging. For great virtuous masters and those possessing the highest Dharma, purple is used; others employ *xiāng* (a pale reddish-brown) color. Brocade, embroidery, painted designs, pearl-encrusted trims, or ornate appliqués are forbidden. Daoist priests and female adepts should always spread a simple, single-layered mat to separate themselves from impurity, distinguishing rank as appropriate. They must carry it with them at all times, never setting it aside. When ascending altars, entering chambers, performing rituals, or attending feasts, they should also bring their incense burner and sitting mat, each taking care to uphold this practice without negligence.

**Chapter on Cultivation Implements: Ruyi**

The *ruyi* was originally a weapon crafted by the Yellow Emperor to battle Chīyóu. Later generations modified it into the *gǔduo*, which celestial perfected beings wield to repel demonic hordes.

**Alchemical Bowl**
The alchemical bowl’s design is ancient, found only among Daoist wanderers and cloud-roaming adepts, typically forged from iron. When entering mountains to gather herbs or lingzhi, where one may remain for days without returning, it serves for cooking rice. For household use in oratories, wooden bowls lacquered with *lài* are employed.

**Yang-Sundial**
In antiquity, the yang-sundial was used to kindle fire for heating incense and invoking spirits. All elixir-refiners employ this fire, never common flame, hence its use among Daoists. For fire arises from the sundial, yet while the sundial is round, the flame is not. The *Cantong Qi* states: “The yang-sundial captures fire; without the sun, no light is born. Direct the sundial toward the sun, place mugwort beneath it, and fire may be obtained.”

**Square Cauldron**
Water is drawn from the square cauldron, yet though the cauldron is square, the water is not. The *Cantong Qi* also notes: “Without stars or moon, how could water be obtained? Its method: direct the cauldron toward the moon, place a cup beneath it, and water may be gathered.” In refining the Great Elixir, Daoists eschew common water, thus employing this water.

**Thunder-Spirit Staff**
While Quanzhen adepts all use plain shovels for ease in removing filth—a practice deemed vulgar and overly mundane—I fashion a *ruyi* from bronze. Its shaft spans six *chǐ*, fitted below with an iron spike and hook for suspending a satchel. Above, a medicine gourd and alchemical scriptures hang from the *ruyi*. This innovation began with me.

**Sleeping Implements**
Sleeping implements are all made of fine cloth or silk.

For Daoist monastics, sleeping quilts must not be made of crimson, purple, azure, green, cloud-patterned silk, *yunbi* damask, brocade, embroidery, pearl-stitched trims, or appliquéd lace—such materials directly violate the precepts and must not be used.

**Mats**
Mats serve to block the noxious *qi* of the earthly corpse. Their size may vary according to individual need. For mats used outdoors, simplicity and elegance suffice.

**Felt**
Felt is not proper attire for Daoist monastics. Since sheep’s wool carries the stench of impurity, it offends the perfected sages; great caution is warranted. When entering the altar to perform Daoist rites, felt must not be worn. Such are the strict regulations of the Daoist code.

**Rain Gear**
Everyday rain gear may be chosen at one’s discretion.

**Quilts**
Quilts should be made of fine, lightweight cloth, without stitched borders or decorative trims. Plainness is prized; extravagance is shunned.

**Sheets**
Sheets—outer coverings—are to be made of fine, lightweight silk, without borders or excessive width.

**Pillows**
Pillows are to be crafted from wood, either plain or lacquered, and shaped as perfect squares. No irregular or curved forms are permitted.

**Wooden Clogs**
The design of wooden clogs embodies the seven *po* souls transformed into dragons and tigers, subdued beneath the soles. Daoist practitioners should wear clogs or ceremonial shoes during worship. For casual movement, clogs allow the seven *po* souls to find repose.

**Clogs**
All who receive the Sevenfold Precepts carry the black *qi* of the three *hun* and five spirits within their robes and headscarves, while the seven *po* and nine numinous spirits dwell beneath their clogs. Thus, advanced Daoists must never allow others to handle their clogs, nor touch their headscarves or robes—this is the reason.

**Indoor Slippers**
Indoor slippers are worn only in bathing chambers for washing the feet; they must not be worn elsewhere.

**Clog Racks**
Since dragons and tigers—divine spirits—lie beneath all clogs, at night they should be placed upon a wooden rack or peachwood stand, never carelessly set on the ground. Daoist practitioners must treat their ritual crowns, clogs, and robes with reverence, never discarding them lightly.

**Clothing Fumigators**
Clothing should always be perfumed, ensuring it remains fragrant and immaculate, so that when one ascends the altar to perform rites, one’s presence harmonizes with the Dao.

**Beds**
For sitting or reclining, beds should have four legs, each eight *chǐ* in length, forming a perfect square, and stand at least three *chǐ* high to sever earthly *qi* and malevolent influences. No carvings or embellishments are permitted. Another tradition speaks of the "Purple Jade Couch of the Three Pristine Ones," a jade bed elevated three *chǐ* above the ground. This height is necessary because the noxious *qi* of the earthly corpse and the five toxins rise a *chǐ* from the ground; without such elevation, the body becomes clouded, the mind’s devotion falters, and communion with the perfected sages is obstructed. Beds are among the most essential furnishings, and their design should prioritize height, brightness, and purity.

**Rope Beds**
Rope beds, or curved benches, are ideal for breathwork and guiding *qi*, offering stability and comfort—an invention of earlier sages. The traditional design, adorned with purple and crimson silk cords and crafted from white sandalwood, is particularly refined. Such is the lofty and pure ethos of the Daoist tradition.

**Mats**
Mats should be made of newly woven material. There are many types of mats, each suited to what is available at the time—reeds, rushes, and the like—all must be clean and fresh. Whether coarse or fine, they may be used as one sees fit.

**Comb Boxes**
Hair is the image of the stars and constellations. It is also called the "dark cloud canopy" and the "remnant of blood." Some say hair is the "jeweled forest of the Purple Palace," the dwelling place of the brain spirit, and thus must be combed and tended with method. Daoists therefore keep a comb box to store their combs.

**Combs**
Combs, with their widely spaced teeth, are essential tools for arranging the hair and promoting the circulation of blood. They must never be neglected for even a moment. Whether young or old, one should comb the hair from beginning to end each day—this is the way to prolong life.

**Fine-Toothed Combs**
Fine-toothed combs, with their closely set teeth, are used to remove filth from the head. They should be made of boxwood, oak, or golden wattle. For those who follow the Dao, materials such as bone, horn, ivory, or tortoiseshell are unsuitable.

**Tweezers**
The nose is the Gate of Heaven, the passage through which *qi* enters and exits. It should always be kept open to allow the free flow of *qi*, so that the spirit may flourish. Tweezers, made of copper or iron, are used to pluck the hairs from the nose.

**Brushes**
Brushes are used to remove the grease and grime from combs and fine-toothed combs. Those made of palm fiber or *Miscanthus* roots are best.

**Ear Spoons**
The ears are the gates of the *hun* and the doors of the *po*. Keeping them clean ensures that the *hun* and *po* remain with the body. The ears are the remnant of skin, and ear spoons—made of gold, silver, copper, iron, bamboo, or ivory—are used to remove the filth from them.

**Nail Clippers**
The gods of the celestial chariot’s guards reside at the tips of the fingernails. Nails are the remnant of sinew, and Daoists clip them on auspicious days to subdue the Three Corpses, preventing the rise of filth and evil. Thus, one must keep a nail clipper for trimming the nails.

**Nail Pouches**
The *Shangqing* scriptures forbid the burning of nails and hair. The *Bowuzhi* states: "The owl gathers people’s nails at night and places them in its eyes to divine the fortune of gold." The *Qianjin Fang* also says: "Nails that enter the abdomen cause illness, which cannot be cured." According to the *Lingshu Jing*, burning nails and hair is among the ten defeats. Some say that ghosts who obtain a person’s nails and hair can bind the three *hun*, leading to nightmares and demonic possession. For these reasons, a nail pouch is indispensable.

**Hair Pouches**
The body, hair, and skin must not be harmed. When combing, loose hair should be gathered and stored in a pouch. If there is much, it should be arranged neatly—never carelessly discarded, as this has been forbidden since ancient times. Those who cultivate the Dao must take heed.

**Fine-Toothed Combs (for Ritual Use)**
Before ascending the altar or entering meditation, one must wash the hands and feet, arrange the hair, and then pay homage. First, a fine-toothed comb is used to smooth the hair before washing. They may be made of ivory, bamboo, or wood, as appropriate.

**Soap Bean Pouches**
There are many methods for removing grease, but for bathing, none surpasses black soybean flour. It should be sewn into a pouch or stored in a box for washing.

**Salt Boxes**
Teeth are the remnant of bone. The mouth and teeth must be regularly cleansed so that all yang energies gather at the head, circulating through the mouth and teeth, thereby naturally connecting with the divine perfected. Moreover, salt congeals the blood, dispels wind, and strengthens the teeth. Thus, it is best to store powdered salt and use it to scrub the teeth frequently.

**Towels**
When combing the hair, one towel should be placed on the knees, and another used to wipe the hands and face.

Scholars of the Dao must regularly cleanse their mouths and teeth, trim their nails and hair, and purify themselves with incense, so that divine spirits may descend and their thoughts may resonate with the celestial. **Deer-Hide Pouches** are used to store medicinal substances; wandering ascetics who roam like clouds and water employ them, for nothing but deer hide is suitable.

**Water Filters**
All water used by Daoists must first be strained before use. These are made of fine silk or gauze. If the material is too coarse, small creatures may slip through, inadvertently harming life.

**Tripods**
The design of tripods need not be elaborated here, for their details are fully recorded in alchemical texts. Those who study the Dao must first master them; there is no need to repeat them here.

**Cauldrons**
In antiquity, there were names such as *golden cauldron*, *Nine Tripods*, and *elixir cauldron*—all used by Daoists for refining elixirs.

**Basins**
Daoist immortals prefer those made of fired clay.

**Jars**
These are the same as those used in common households, but they must be kept clean and free from meat, fish, grease, and oil.

**Steamers**
There are names such as *golden cauldron and jade steamer* among those who cultivate immortality. The *Erya* states: "A steamer is called a *yan*." It is also known simply as a *zeng*. In the capital and Luoyang, they are made of pottery—essentially basins without bottoms. In Jianghuai and the Wu-Chu regions, they are made of wood, while in Lingbiao, some are fashioned from large bamboo fungi, used for steaming medicinal substances. Such implements are indispensable in Daoist practice.

**Vases**
The Daoist scriptures speak of *Jade Capital and Golden Vases*. In the capital and Luoyang, smaller jars are called *vases*. *Vase* is another name for *jar* or *urn*, and its use among people is especially indispensable.

**Trays**
Modern trays are generally large and square, with tooth-like feet called *tooth trays*. These are stable and convenient for offerings. Large round trays with feet are also excellent. Their surfaces are often adorned with painted clouds, coiling dragons, dancing phoenixes, white cranes, and soaring luan birds.

**Offering Boxes**
In contemporary usage, those carried in the hands are called *qing*, while those with lids that fit over one another are called *boxes*. Both serve as offering vessels before the Heavenly Worthies, and their varieties are numerous.

**Pouring Bowls**
Wherever Daoist adepts or female immortals reside, they must first prepare washing basins and pouring bowls, or vessels to hold clean water. In all actions and movements, they must rinse their mouths and wash their hands. These vessels may be made of gold, silver, bronze, or pottery, according to what is available, but they must always have lids to prevent wind, dust, insects, or impurities from contaminating them. Such purity far surpasses that of ordinary vessels.

**Spittoons**
When ascending the altar or entering the meditation chamber, one must rinse the mouth with clean water and spit into a bowl or tray, never onto the ground. For to serve Heaven is like serving a sovereign, and to serve one’s master is like serving the divine. Before scriptures and images, one must not spit, rinse the mouth, or raise one’s voice loudly, but always conduct oneself as if in the presence of a wise ruler. If even serving one’s master demands such reverence, how much more so before scriptures and images!
